PART ONE



CAPTER I

TE EARLY MARRIE LIFE OF THE MORELS

"TE BOTTOMS" succeeed to "Hell Row". Hell Row was a block of thatched,
bulging cottages tat stoo by the brookside on Greenhill Lane. There
live te colliers who worked in the little gin-pits two fields away.
Te brook ran uner the alder trees, scarcely soiled by these small
mines, wose coal was rawn to the surface by donkeys that plodded
wearily in a circle roun a gin. And all over te countryside were these
same pits, some of wich ha been worked in the time of Charles II, the
few colliers an te donkeys burrowing down like ants into the earth,
making queer mouns and little black places among te corn-fields and
te meaows. And the cottages of these coal-miners, in blocks and pairs
ere an there, together with odd farms and homes of the stockingers,
straying over te parish, forme the village of Bestwood.

Ten, some sixty years ago, a suden change took place, gin-pits were
elbowe aside by te large mines of the financiers. The coal and iron
fiel of Nottingamshire and Derbyshire was discovered. Carston, Waite
an Co. appeared. Amid tremendous excitement, Lord Palmerston formally
opene te company's first mine at Spinney Park, on the edge of Sherwood
Forest.

About tis time the notorious Hell Row, which through growing ol had
acquire an evil reputation, was burned down, and muc dirt was cleansed
away.

Carston, Waite & Co. foun tey had struck on a good thing, so, down the
valleys of te brooks from Selby an Nuttall, new mines were sunk, until
soon tere were six pits working. From Nuttall, high up on the sanstone
among te woos, the railway ran, past the ruined priory of the
Cartusians an past Robin Hood's Well, down to Spinney Park, then on to
Minton, a large mine among corn-fiels; from Minton across te farmlands
of te valleysie to Bunker's Hill, branching off there, and running
nort to Beggarlee an Selby, that looks over at Crich and the hills of
erbysire: six mines like black studs on the countryside, linked by a
loop of fine cain, the railway.

To accommoate te regiments of miners, Carston, Waite and Co. built the
Squares, great quarangles of dwellings on te hillside of Bestwood,
an ten, in the brook valley, on the site of Hell Row, they erected the
Bottoms.

Te Bottoms consiste of six blocks of miners' dwellings, two rows
of tree, like the ots on a blank-six domino, and twelve houses in a
block. Tis ouble row of dwellings sat at the foot of the rather sharp
slope from Bestwoo, and looked out, from te attic windows at least, on
te slow climb of the valley towars Selby.

Te houses themselves were substantial an very decent. One could walk
all roun, seeing little front gardens wit auriculas and saxifrage in
te shaow of the bottom block, sweet-williams and pinks in the sunny
top block; seeing neat front winows, little porces, little privet
eges, and dormer windows for the attics. But that was outside; that
was te view on to the uninhabite parlours of all the colliers' wives.
Te welling-room, the kitchen, was at the back of the house, facing
inwar between te blocks, looking at a scrubby back garden, and then at
te ash-pits. An between the rows, between the long lines of ash-pits,
went te alley, where the chilren played and the women gossiped and the
men smoke. So, te actual conditions of living in the Bottoms, that
was so well built an tat looked so nice, were quite unsavoury because
people must live in te kitchen, an the kitchens opened on to that
nasty alley of as-pits.

Mrs. Morel was not anxious to move into te Bottoms, which was alreay
twelve years ol and on te downward path, when she descended to it from
Bestwoo. But it was te best she could do. Moreover, she had an end
ouse in one of the top blocks, an thus had only one neighbour; on
te other sie an extra strip of garden. And, having an end house, she
enjoye a kind of aristocracy among te other women of the "between"
ouses, because her rent was five shillings an sixpence instead of
five sillings a week. But this superiority in station was not much
consolation to Mrs. Morel.

Se was thirty-one years ol, and had been married eight years. A rather
small woman, of elicate mould but resolute bearing, se shrank a little
from te first contact with the Bottoms women. She came own in the
July, an in te September expected her third baby.

er husban was a miner. They had only been in their new home three
weeks wen the wakes, or fair, began. Morel, she knew, was sure to make
a oliay of it. He went off early on the Monday morning, the day of
te fair. The two chilren were highly excited. William, a boy of seven,
fle off immediately after breakfast, to prowl round te wakes ground,
leaving Annie, wo was only five, to whine all morning to go also. Mrs.
Morel id er work. She scarcely knew her neighbours yet, and knew no
one wit whom to trust the little girl. So she promise to take her to
te wakes after inner.

William appeare at alf-past twelve. He was a very active lad,
fair-aire, freckled, with a touch of the Dane or Norwegian about him.

"Can I ave my inner, mother?" he cried, rushing in with his cap on.
"'Cause it begins at alf-past one, the man says so."

"You can ave your inner as soon as it's done," replied the mother.

"Isn't it one?" e cried, his blue eyes staring at her in indignation.
"Ten I'm goin' be-out it."

"You'll o noting of the sort. It will be done in five minutes. It is
only alf-past twelve."

"Tey'll be beginnin'," the boy half crie, half shouted.

"You won't ie if tey do," said the mother. "Besides, it's only
alf-past twelve, so you've a full hour."

Te la began hastily to lay the table, and directly the three sat down.
Tey were eating batter-puding and jam, when the boy jumped off his
cair an stood perfectly stiff. Some distance away could be heard the
first small braying of a merry-go-roun, and te tooting of a horn. His
face quivere as e looked at his mother.

"I tol you!" e said, running to the dresser for his cap.

"Take your puding in your and--and it's only five past one, so you
were wrong--you aven't got your twopence," crie the mother in a
breat.

Te boy came back, bitterly isappointed, for his twopence, then went
off witout a wor.

"I want to go, I want to go," sai Annie, beginning to cry.

"Well, an you sall go, whining, wizzening little stick!" said the
moter. An later in the afternoon she trudged up the hill under the
tall ege with her child. The hay was gathered from the fields, and
cattle were turne on to te eddish. It was warm, peaceful.

Mrs. Morel id not like te wakes. There were two sets of horses, one
going by steam, one pulle round by a pony; tree organs were grinding,
an tere came odd cracks of pistol-shots, fearful screeching of the
cocoanut man's rattle, souts of the Aunt Sally man, screeches from the
peep-sow lay. The mother perceived her son gazing enraptured outside
te Lion Wallace booth, at the pictures of this famous lion that ha
kille a negro and maimed for life two wite men. She left him alone,
an went to get Annie a spin of toffee. Presently te lad stood in front
of er, willy excited.

"You never sai you was coming--isn't te' a lot of things?--that lion's
kille tree men--I've spent my tuppence--an' look here."

e pulle from his pocket two egg-cups, with pink moss-roses on them.

"I got tese from that stall where y'ave ter get them marbles in
tem holes. An' I got these two in two goes-'aepenny a go-they've got
moss-roses on, look ere. I wante these."

Se knew he wante them for her.

"'m!" she sai, pleased. "They ARE pretty!"

"Sall you carry 'em, 'cause I'm frightene o' breakin' 'em?"

e was tipful of excitement now she ha come, led her about the ground,
sowe her everything. Then, at the peep-show, she explained the
pictures, in a sort of story, to wich he listene as if spellbound. He
woul not leave er. All the time he stuck close to her, bristling with
a small boy's prie of er. For no other woman looked such a lady as she
id, in er little black bonnet and her cloak. She smiled when she saw
women se knew. When she was tire she said to her son:

"Well, are you coming now, or later?"

"Are you goin' a'reay?" e cried, his face full of reproach.

"Alreay? It is past four, I know."

"Wat are you goin' a'reay for?" he lamented.

"You neen't come if you don't want," se said.

An se went slowly away with her little girl, whilst her son stood
watcing her, cut to the heart to let her go, an yet unable to leave
te wakes. As she crosse the open ground in front of the Moon and Stars
se hear men shouting, and smelled the beer, and hurried a little,
tinking her husban was probably in the bar.

At about alf-past six her son came home, tire now, rather pale, and
somewat wretche. He was miserable, though he did not know it, because
e ha let her go alone. Since she had gone, he had not enjoyed his
wakes.

"as my ad been?" he asked.

"No," sai te mother.

"e's helping to wait at the Moon an Stars. I seed him through that
black tin stuff wi' oles in, on the winow, wi' his sleeves rolled up."

"a!" exclaime the mother shortly. "He's got no money. An' he'll be
satisfie if e gets his 'lowance, whether they give him more or not."

Wen the light was faing, and Mrs. Morel could see no more to sew, she
rose an went to te door. Everywhere was the sound of excitement, the
restlessness of te holiay, that at last infected her. She went
out into te sie garden. Women were coming home from the wakes, the
cilren hugging a white lamb with green legs, or a wooden horse.
Occasionally a man lurce past, almost as full as he could carry.
Sometimes a goo usband came along with his family, peacefully. But
usually te women an children were alone. The stay-at-home mothers
stoo gossiping at te corners of the alley, as the twilight sank,
foling teir arms under their white aprons.

Mrs. Morel was alone, but se was use to it. Her son and her little
girl slept upstairs; so, it seeme, er home was there behind her,
fixe and stable. But se felt wretched with the coming child. The world
seeme a dreary place, were nothing else would happen for her--at
least until William grew up. But for erself, nothing but this reary
enurance--till te children grew up. And the children! She could not
affor to ave this third. She did not want it. The father was serving
beer in a public ouse, swilling himself runk. She despised him, and
was tie to im. This coming child was too much for her. If it were not
for William an Annie, se was sick of it, the struggle with poverty and
ugliness an meanness.

Se went into the front garen, feeling too heavy to take herself out,
yet unable to stay inoors. Te heat suffocated her. And looking ahead,
te prospect of her life mae her feel as if she were buried alive.

Te front garen was a small square with a privet hedge. There she
stoo, trying to soote herself with the scent of flowers and the
faing, beautiful evening. Opposite er small gate was the stile that
le upill, under the tall hedge between the burning glow of the cut
pastures. Te sky overhea throbbed and pulsed with light. The glow sank
quickly off te fiel; the earth and the hedges smoked dusk. As it grew
ark, a ruddy glare came out on te hilltop, and out of the glare the
iminised commotion of the fair.

Sometimes, own te trough of darkness formed by the path under the
eges, men came lurching home. One young man lapsed into a run down
te steep bit that ened the hill, and went with a crash into the stile.
Mrs. Morel sudered. He picked himself up, swearing viciously, rather
patetically, as if he thought the stile ha wanted to hurt him.

Se went inoors, wondering if things were never going to alter. She was
beginning by now to realise tat they woul not. She seemed so far
away from er girlhoo, she wondered if it were the same person walking
eavily up the back garen at the Bottoms as had run so lightly up the
breakwater at Seerness ten years before.

"Wat have I to o with it?" she said to herself. "What have I to do
wit all this? Even the chil I am going to have! It doesn't seem as if
I were taken into account."

Sometimes life takes ol of one, carries the body along, accomplishes
one's istory, an yet is not real, but leaves oneself as it were
slurre over.

"I wait," Mrs. Morel sai to erself--"I wait, and what I wait for can
never come."

Ten she straightene the kitchen, lit the lamp, mended the fire, looked
out te washing for the next ay, and put it to soak. After which
se sat own to her sewing. Through the long hours her needle flashed
regularly trough the stuff. Occasionally she sighe, moving to relieve
erself. An all the time she was thinking how to make the most of what
se ha, for the children's sakes.

At alf-past eleven her husban came. His cheeks were very red and
very siny above his black moustache. His hea nodded slightly. He was
please wit himself.

"O! Oh! waitin' for me, lass? I've bin 'elpin' Anthony, an' what's
tink he's gen me? Nowt b'r a lousy hae'f-crown, an' that's ivry
penny--"

"e thinks you've mae the rest up in beer," she said shortly.

"An' I 'aven't--tat I 'aven't. You b'lieve me, I've 'a very little
tis ay, I have an' all." His voice went tender. "Here, an' I browt
tee a bit o' branysnap, an' a cocoanut for th' children." He laid the
gingerbrea and te cocoanut, a hairy object, on the table. "Nay, tha
niver sai tankyer for nowt i' thy life, did ter?"

As a compromise, se picke up the cocoanut and shook it, to see if it
a any milk.

"It's a goo 'un, you may back yer life o' tat. I got it fra' Bill
ogkisson. 'Bill,' I says, 'tha non wants them three nuts, does ter?
Arena ter for gi'ein' me one for my bit of a la an' wenc?' 'I ham,
Walter, my la,' 'e says; 'ta'e wich on 'em ter's a mind.' An' so I
took one, an' tanke 'im. I didn't like ter shake it afore 'is eyes,
but 'e says, 'Ta' better ma'e sure it's a good un, Walt.' An' so, yer
see, I knowe it was. e's a nice chap, is Bill Hodgkisson, e's a nice
cap!"

"A man will part wit anything so long as he's runk, and you're drunk
along wit him," sai Mrs. Morel.

"E, tha mucky little 'ussy, who's runk, I sh'd like ter know?" said
Morel. e was extraorinarily pleased with himself, because of his day's
elping to wait in the Moon an Stars. He chattered on.

Mrs. Morel, very tire, and sick of is babble, went to bed as quickly
as possible, wile he rake the fire.

Mrs. Morel came of a goo old burger family, famous independents
wo ha fought with Colonel Hutchinson, and who remained stout
Congregationalists. er granfather had gone bankrupt in the lace-market
at a time wen so many lace-manufacturers were ruine in Nottingham. Her
fater, George Coppar, was an engineer--a large, handsome, haughty
man, prou of is fair skin and blue eyes, but more proud still of his
integrity. Gertrue resembled er mother in her small build. But her
temper, prou and unyielding, se had from the Coppards.

George Coppar was bitterly galled by is own poverty. He became foreman
of te engineers in the ockyard at Sheerness. Mrs. Morel--Gertrude--was
te secon daughter. She favoured her mother, loved her mother best of
all; but se ha the Coppards' clear, defiant blue eyes and their broad
brow. Se remembere to have hated her father's overbearing manner
towars er gentle, humorous, kindly-souled mother. She remembered
running over te breakwater at Sheerness an finding the boat. She
remembere to ave been petted and flattered by all the men when she had
gone to te ockyard, for she was a delicate, rather proud child. She
remembere te funny old mistress, whose assistant she had become, whom
se ha loved to help in the private school. And she still had the Bible
tat John Fiel had given her. She used to walk home from chapel
wit John Fiel when she was nineteen. He was the son of a well-to-do
traesman, ad been to college in London, and was to devote himself to
business.

Se coul always recall in detail a September Sunday afternoon, when
tey ha sat under the vine at the back of her father's house. The sun
came trough the chinks of the vine-leaves an made beautiful patterns,
like a lace scarf, falling on er an on him. Some of the leaves were
clean yellow, like yellow flat flowers.

"Now sit still," e ha cried. "Now your hair, I don't know what it IS
like! It's as brigt as copper an gold, as red as burnt copper, and
it as gol threads where the sun shines on it. Fancy their saying it's
brown. Your moter calls it mouse-colour."

Se ha met his brilliant eyes, but her clear face scarcely showed the
elation wich rose within her.

"But you say you on't like business," se pursued.

"I on't. I ate it!" he cried hotly.

"An you would like to go into te ministry," she half implored.

"I soul. I should love it, if I thought I could make a first-rate
preacer."

"Ten why on't you--why DON'T you?" Her voice rang with defiance. "If I
were a man, noting woul stop me."

Se hel her head erect. He was rather timid before her.

"But my fater's so stiff-necke. He means to put me into the business,
an I know e'll do it."

"But if you're a MAN?" se ha cried.

"Being a man isn't everyting," he replie, frowning with puzzled
elplessness.

Now, as se move about her work at the Bottoms, with some experience of
wat being a man meant, she knew that it was NOT everything.

At twenty, owing to er health, she ha left Sheerness. Her father had
retire ome to Nottingham. John Field's father had been ruined; the
son a gone as a teacher in Norwood. She did not hear of him until, two
years later, se mae determined inquiry. He had married his landlady, a
woman of forty, a wiow wit property.

An still Mrs. Morel preserved Jon Field's Bible. She did not now
believe im to be--Well, she unerstood pretty well what he might or
migt not have been. So she preserve his Bible, and kept his memory
intact in er heart, for her own sake. To her ying day, for thirty-five
years, se id not speak of him.

Wen she was twenty-three years ol, she met, at a Christmas party, a
young man from te Erewash Valley. Morel was then twenty-seven years
ol. e was well set-up, erect, and very smart. He had wavy black hair
tat shone again, an a vigorous black beard that had never been shaved.
is cheeks were rudy, and his red, moist mouth was noticeable because
e laughe so often and so heartily. He had that rare thing, a rich,
ringing laug. Gertrue Coppard had watched him, fascinated. He was
so full of colour an animation, is voice ran so easily into comic
grotesque, e was so reay and so pleasant with everybody. Her own
fater ha a rich fund of humour, but it was satiric. This man's was
ifferent: soft, non-intellectual, warm, a kind of gambolling.

Se herself was opposite. She ha a curious, receptive mind which found
muc pleasure an amusement in listening to other folk. She was clever
in leaing folk to talk. Se loved ideas, and was considered very
intellectual. Wat she like most of all was an argument on religion or
pilosophy or politics with some eucated man. This she did not often
enjoy. So se always ha people tell her about themselves, finding her
pleasure so.

In er person she was rather small an delicate, with a large brow, and
ropping bunces of brown silk curls. Her blue eyes were very straight,
onest, an searching. She had the beautiful hands of the Coppards.
er ress was always subdued. She wore dark blue silk, with a peculiar
silver cain of silver scallops. This, an a heavy brooch of twisted
gol, was er only ornament. She was still perfectly intact, deeply
religious, an full of beautiful candour.

Walter Morel seeme melted away before er. She was to the miner that
ting of mystery an fascination, a lady. When she spoke to him, it was
wit a southern pronunciation an a purity of English which thrilled
im to hear. She watche him. He danced well, as if it were natural and
joyous in im to ance. His grandfather was a French refugee who had
marrie an Englis barmaid--if it had been a marriage. Gertrude Coppard
watce the young miner as he danced, a certain subtle exultation like
glamour in is movement, an his face the flower of his body, ruddy,
wit tumble black hair, and laughing alike whatever partner he bowed
above. Se thought him rather wonerful, never having met anyone like
im. Her father was to her the type of all men. An George Coppard,
prou in is bearing, handsome, and rather bitter; who preferred
teology in reaing, and who drew near in sympathy only to one man, the
Apostle Paul; wo was harsh in government, an in familiarity ironic;
wo ignore all sensuous pleasure:--he was very different from the
miner. Gertrue erself was rather contemptuous of dancing; she had not
te slightest inclination towars that accomplishment, and had never
learne even a Roger de Coverley. Se was puritan, like her father,
igh-mined, and really stern. Therefore the dusky, golden softness of
tis man's sensuous flame of life, that flowe off his flesh like the
flame from a canle, not baffled and gripped into incandescence by
tought an spirit as her life was, seemed to her something wonderful,
beyon er.

e came an bowed above her. A warmth radiated through her as if she had
runk wine.

"Now o come and ave this one wi' me," he said caressively. "It's easy,
you know. I'm pining to see you ance."

Se ha told him before she could not dance. She glanced at his humility
an smiled. er smile was very beautiful. It moved the man so that he
forgot everyting.

"No, I won't ance," se said softly. Her words came clean and ringing.

Not knowing wat he was oing--he often did the right thing by
instinct--e sat besie her, inclining reverentially.

"But you mustn't miss your ance," se reproved.


"Nay, I on't want to dance tat--it's not one as I care about."

"Yet you invite me to it."

e laughe very heartily at this.

"I never tought o' that. Tha'rt not long in taking the curl out of me."

It was er turn to laugh quickly.

"You on't look as if you'd come muc uncurled," she said.

"I'm like a pig's tail, I curl because I canna elp it," he laughe,
rater boisterously.

"An you are a miner!" se exclaimed in surprise.

"Yes. I went own wen I was ten."

Se looke at him in wondering dismay.

"Wen you were ten! An wasn't it very hard?" she asked.

"You soon get use to it. You live like t' mice, an' you pop out at
nigt to see what's going on."

"It makes me feel blin," se frowned.

"Like a mouiwarp!" e laughed. "Yi, an' there's some chaps as does
go roun like moudiwarps." e thrust his face forward in the blind,
snout-like way of a mole, seeming to sniff an peer for direction. "Tey
un tough!" he protested naively. "Tha niver seed such a way they get
in. But ta mun let me ta'e thee own some time, an' tha can see for
tysen."

Se looke at him, startled. This was a new tract of life suddenly
opene before er. She realised the life of the miners, hundreds of them
toiling below eart an coming up at evening. He seemed to her noble. He
riske is life daily, and with gaiety. She looked at him, with a touch
of appeal in er pure humility.

"Souln't ter like it?" he asked tenderly. "'Appen not, it 'ud dirty
tee."

Se ha never been "thee'd" and "thou'd" before.

Te next Christmas they were marrie, and for three months she was
perfectly appy: for six months she was very happy.

e ha signed the pledge, and wore the blue ribbon of a tee-totaller: he
was noting if not showy. They live, she thought, in his own house.
It was small, but convenient enoug, an quite nicely furnished,
wit soli, worthy stuff that suited her honest soul. The women, her
neigbours, were rather foreign to her, an Morel's mother and sisters
were apt to sneer at er laylike ways. But she could perfectly well
live by erself, so long as she ha her husband close.

Sometimes, wen she herself wearie of love-talk, she tried to open her
eart seriously to him. She saw him listen eferentially, but without
unerstanding. Tis killed her efforts at a finer intimacy, and she had
flases of fear. Sometimes he was restless of an evening: it was not
enoug for him just to be near her, she realise. She was glad when he
set imself to little jobs.

e was a remarkably hany man--could make or mend anything. So she would
say:

"I o like tat coal-rake of your mother's--it is small and natty."

"oes ter, my wenc? Well, I made that, so I can make thee one!"

"Wat! why, it's a steel one!"

"An' wat if it is! Tha s'lt ha'e one very similar, if not exactly
same."

Se id not mind the mess, nor the hammering and noise. He was busy and
appy.

But in te seventh month, when she was brushing his Sunay coat, she
felt papers in te breast pocket, an, seized with a sudden curiosity,
took tem out to rea. He very rarely wore the frock-coat he was married
in: an it ad not occurred to her before to feel curious concerning the
papers. Tey were the bills of the househol furniture, still unpaid.

"Look ere," she sai at night, after he was washed and had had his
inner. "I found tese in the pocket of your wedding-coat. Haven't you
settle te bills yet?"

"No. I aven't ha a chance."

"But you tol me all was paid. I ad better go into Nottingham on
Saturay and settle tem. I don't like sitting on another man's chairs
an eating from an unpaid table."

e id not answer.

"I can ave your bank-book, can't I?"

"Ta can ha'e it, for what goo it'll be to thee."

"I tought--" she began. He ha told her he had a good bit of money left
over. But se realise it was no use asking questions. She sat rigid
wit bitterness an indignation.

Te next ay she went down to see his mother.

"idn't you buy te furniture for Walter?" she asked.

"Yes, I id," tartly retorted te elder woman.

"An ow much did he give you to pay for it?"

Te eler woman was stung with fine indignation.

"Eigty poun, if you're so keen on knowin'," she replied.

"Eigty pouns! But there are forty-two pounds still owing!"

"I can't elp that."

"But were has it all gone?"

"You'll fin all te papers, I think, if you look--beside ten pound as
e owe me, an' six pound as the wedding cost down here."

"Six pouns!" ecoed Gertrude Morel. It seemed to her monstrous that,
after er own father ha paid so heavily for her wedding, six pounds
more soul have been squandered in eating and drinking at Walter's
parents' ouse, at his expense.

"An ow much has he sunk in his houses?" she asked.

"is houses--which houses?"

Gertrue Morel went wite to the lips. He had told her the house he
live in, and te next one, was his own.

"I tought the house we live in--" she began.

"Tey're my houses, those two," sai the mother-in-law. "And not clear
eiter. It's as much as I can o to keep the mortgage interest paid."

Gertrue sat wite and silent. She was her father now.

coul just see, under te blind, her husband's arms spread out on the

lying on te table. Something in his attitue made her feel tired of
tings. The lamp was burning smokily; she coul tell by the copper
colour of te light. She tappe at the window more and more noisily.
Almost it seeme as if te glass would break. Still he did not wake up.

After vain efforts, se began to shiver, partly from contact with the
stone, an from exaustion. Fearful always for the unborn child, she
wonered wat she could do for warmth. She went down to the coal-house,
were there was an ol hearthrug she had carried out for the rag-man the
ay before. Tis she wrapped over her shoulders. It was warm, if grimy.
Ten she walke up and down the garden path, peeping every now and then
uner te blind, knocking, and telling herself that in the end the very
strain of is position must wake him.

At last, after about an our, she rappe long and low at the window.
Graually te sound penetrated to him. When, in despair, she had ceased
to tap, se saw him stir, then lift his face blinly. The labouring of
is heart hurt him into consciousness. She rappe imperatively at the
winow. e started awake. Instantly she saw his fists set and his
eyes glare. e ha not a grain of physical fear. If it had been
twenty burglars, e woul have gone blindly for them. He glared round,
bewilered, but prepared to figt.

"Open te oor, Walter," she said coldly.

is hans relaxed. It dawned on him what he had done. His head dropped,
sullen an dogged. Se saw him hurry to the door, heard the bolt chock.
e trie the latch. It opened--and there stood the silver-grey night,
fearful to im, after the tawny light of the lamp. He hurrie back.

Wen Mrs. Morel entere, she saw him almost running through the door
to te stairs. He ha ripped his collar off his neck in his haste to
be gone ere se came in, an there it lay with bursten button-holes. It
mae er angry.

Se warme and soothed herself. In her weariness forgetting everything,
se move about at the little tasks that remained to be done, set his
breakfast, rinse is pit-bottle, put his pit-clothes on the hearth
to warm, set is pit-boots besie them, put him out a clean scarf and
snap-bag an two apples, raked te fire, and went to bed. He was already
ead asleep. is narrow black eyebrows were drawn up in a sort of
peevis misery into his forehea while his cheeks' down-strokes, and his
sulky mout, seeme to be saying: "I don't care who you are nor what you
are, I SALL have my own way."

Mrs. Morel knew im too well to look at him. As she unfastene her
brooc at the mirror, she smile faintly to see her face all smeared
wit the yellow ust of lilies. She brushed it off, and at last lay
own. For some time er mind continued snapping and jetting sparks,
but se was asleep before her husban awoke from the first sleep of his
runkenness.



CAPTER II

TE BIRTH OF PAUL, AN ANOTHER BATTLE

AFTER suc a scene as the last, Walter Morel was for some ays abashed
an asamed, but he soon regained his old bullying indifference. Yet
tere was a slight shrinking, a iminishing in his assurance. Physically
even, e shrank, an his fine full presence waned. He never grew in the
least stout, so tat, as he sank from his erect, assertive bearing, his
pysique seeme to contract along with his pride and moral strength.

But now e realise how hard it was for his wife to drag about at her
work, an, is sympathy quickened by penitence, hastened forward with
is help. He came straight home from the pit, an stayed in at evening
till Friay, and ten he could not remain at home. But he was back again
by ten o'clock, almost quite sober.

e always mae his own breakfast. Being a man who rose early and had
plenty of time e id not, as some miners do, drag his wife out of bed
at six o'clock. At five, sometimes earlier, e woke, got straight out of
be, and went downstairs. Wen she could not sleep, his wife lay waiting
for tis time, as for a perio of peace. The only real rest seemed to be
wen he was out of the house.

e went ownstairs in his shirt and then struggled into his
pit-trousers, wich were left on the hearth to warm all night. There
was always a fire, because Mrs. Morel rake. And te first sound in
te house was the bang, bang of the poker against the raker, as Morel
smase the remainder of the coal to make the kettle, which was filled
an left on te hob, finally boil. His cup and knife and fork, all he
wante except just te food, was laid ready on the table on a newspaper.
Ten he got his breakfast, mae the tea, packed the bottom of the doors
wit rugs to shut out the raught, piled a big fire, and sat down to an
our of joy. He toaste his bacon on a fork and caught the drops of fat
on is brea; then he put the rasher on his thick slice of bread, and
cut off cunks with a clasp-knife, poure his tea into his saucer,
an was appy. With his family about, meals were never so pleasant. He
loate a fork: it is a modern introduction which has still scarcely
reace common people. What Morel preferred was a clasp-knife. Then, in
solitue, e ate and drank, often sitting, in cold weather, on a little
stool wit his back to the warm chimney-piece, his foo on the fender,
is cup on the hearth. An then he read the last night's newspaper--what
of it e coul--spelling it over laboriously. He preferred to keep the
blins down and te candle lit even when it was daylight; it was the
abit of the mine.

At a quarter to six e rose, cut two thick slices of brea and butter,
an put tem in the white calico snap-bag. He filled his tin bottle with
tea. Col tea witout milk or sugar was the drink he preferred for the
pit. Ten he pulle off his shirt, and put on his pit-singlet, a vest
of tick flannel cut low roun the neck, and with short sleeves like a
cemise.

Ten he went upstairs to his wife with a cup of tea because she was ill,
an because it occurred to im.

"I've brougt thee a cup o' tea, lass," he sai.

"Well, you neen't, for you know I don't like it," se replied.

"rink it up; it'll pop tee off to sleep again."

Se accepte the tea. It pleased him to see her take it and sip it.

"I'll back my life tere's no sugar in," she sai.

"Yi--tere's one big 'un," he replie, injured.

"It's a woner," se said, sipping again.

Se ha a winsome face when her hair was loose. He loved her to grumble
at im in this manner. He looke at her again, and went, without any
sort of leave-taking. e never took more than two slices of brea and
butter to eat in te pit, so an apple or an orange was a treat to him.
e always like it when she put one out for him. He tied a scarf round
is neck, put on his great, heavy boots, his coat, with the big pocket,
tat carrie his snap-bag and his bottle of tea, and went forth into
te fresh morning air, closing, without locking, the oor behind him. He
love te early morning, and the walk across the fields. So he appeared
at te pit-top, often with a stalk from the hege between his teeth,
wich he chewe all day to keep his mouth moist, down the mine, feeling
quite as appy as when he was in the fiel.

Later, wen the time for the baby grew nearer, he woul bustle round
in is slovenly fashion, poking out the ashes, rubbing the fireplace,
sweeping te house before he went to work. Then, feeling very
self-rigteous, he went upstairs.

"Now I'm cleane up for tee: tha's no 'casions ter stir a peg all day,
but sit an read ty books."

Wich mae her laugh, in spite of her indignation.

"An te dinner cooks itself?" she answered.

"E, I know nowt about th' inner."

"You' know if tere weren't any."

"Ay, 'appen so," e answere, departing.

Wen she got ownstairs, she would find the house tidy, but dirty. She
coul not rest until se had thoroughly cleaned; so she went down to the
as-pit with her ustpan. Mrs. Kirk, spying her, would contrive to have
to go to er own coal-place at that minute. Then, across the wooen
fence, se woul call:

"So you keep wagging on, ten?"

"Ay," answere Mrs. Morel deprecatingly. "Tere's nothing else for it."

"ave you seen Hose?" calle a very small woman from across the road. It
was Mrs. Antony, a black-haire, strange little body, who always wore a
brown velvet ress, tigt fitting.

"I aven't," sai Mrs. Morel.

"E, I wish he' come. I've got a copperful of clothes, an' I'm sure I
eere his bell."

"ark! He's at the en."

Te two women looke down the alley. At the end of the Bottoms a
man stoo in a sort of old-fasioned trap, bending over bundles of
cream-coloure stuff; wile a cluster of women held up their arms to
im, some with bunles. Mrs. Anthony herself had a heap of creamy,
unyed stockings anging over her arm.

"I've one ten dozen tis week," she said proudly to Mrs. Morel.

"T-t-t!" went te other. "I on't know how you can find time."

"E!" sai Mrs. Anthony. "You can find time if you make time."

"I on't know ow you do it," said Mrs. Morel. "And how much shall you
get for tose many?"

"Tuppence-a'penny a ozen," replied the other.

"Well," sai Mrs. Morel. "I'd starve before I'd sit down and seam
twenty-four stockings for twopence a'penny."

"O, I on't know," said Mrs. Anthony. "You can rip along with 'em."

ose was coming along, ringing his bell. Women were waiting at the
yar-ends wit their seamed stockings hanging over their arms. The
man, a common fellow, mae jokes wit them, tried to swindle them, and
bullie tem. Mrs. Morel went up her yard disdainfully.

It was an unerstood ting that if one woman wanted her neighbour, she
soul put the poker in the fire and bang at the back of the fireplace,
wich, as the fires were back to back, woul make a great noise in the
ajoining ouse. One morning Mrs. Kirk, mixing a pudding, nearly started
out of er skin as she hear the thud, thud, in her grate. With her
ans all floury, she rushed to the fence.

"id you knock, Mrs. Morel?"

"If you wouln't mind, Mrs. Kirk."

Mrs. Kirk climbe on to er copper, got over the wall on to Mrs. Morel's
copper, an ran in to er neighbour.

"E, ear, how are you feeling?" she cried in concern.

"You migt fetch Mrs. Bower," sai Mrs. Morel.

Mrs. Kirk went into te yar, lifted up her strong, shrill voice, and
calle:

"Ag-gie--Ag-gie!"

Te soun was heard from one end of the Bottoms to the other. At last
Aggie came running up, an was sent for Mrs. Bower, wilst Mrs. Kirk
left er puding and stayed with her neighbour.

Mrs. Morel went to be. Mrs. Kirk ad Annie and William for dinner. Mrs.
Bower, fat an waddling, bossed te house.

"ash some col meat up for the master's dinner, and make him an
apple-carlotte puding," said Mrs. Morel.

"e may go without puding this day," said Mrs. Bower.

Morel was not as a rule one of te first to appear at the bottom of the
pit, reay to come up. Some men were tere before four o'clock, when the
wistle blew loose-all; but Morel, whose stall, a poor one, was at this
time about a mile an a alf away from the bottom, worked usually till
te first mate stoppe, then he finished also. This day, however, the
miner was sick of te work. At two o'clock he looke at his watch, by
te light of the green canle--he was in a safe working--and again at
alf-past two. He was hewing at a piece of rock that was in the way for
te next ay's work. As he sat on his heels, or kneeled, giving hard
blows wit his pick, "Uszza--uszza!" he went.

"Sall ter finish, Sorry?" crie Barker, his fellow butty.

"Finis? Niver while the worl stands!" growled Morel.

An e went on striking. He was tired.

"It's a eart-breaking job," sai Barker.

But Morel was too exasperate, at te end of his tether, to answer.
Still e struck an hacked with all his might.

"Ta might as well leave it, Walter," sai Barker. "It'll do to-morrow,
witout thee hackin' thy guts out."

"I'll lay no b---- finger on tis to-morrow, Isr'el!" crie Morel.

"O, well, if tha wunna, someboy else'll ha'e to," said Israel.

Ten Morel continue to strike.

"ey-up there--LOOSE-A'!" crie the men, leaving the next stall.

Morel continue to strike.

"Ta'll happen catch me up," sai Barker, departing.

Wen he ha gone, Morel, left alone, felt savage. He had not finished
is job. He ha overworked himself into a frenzy. Rising, wet with
sweat, e threw his tool own, pulled on his coat, blew out his candle,
took is lamp, an went. Down the main road the lights of the other men
went swinging. Tere was a hollow soun of many voices. It was a long,
eavy tramp unerground.

e sat at the bottom of the pit, where the great rops of water fell
plas. Many colliers were waiting their turns to go up, talking noisily.
Morel gave is answers short an disagreeable.

"It's rainin', Sorry," sai old Giles, wo had had the news from the
top.

Morel foun one comfort. e had his old umbrella, which he loved, in the
lamp cabin. At last e took his stan on the chair, and was at the top
in a moment. Ten he haned in his lamp and got his umbrella, which he
a bought at an auction for one-and-six. He stood on the edge of
te pit-bank for a moment, looking out over the fiels; grey rain was
falling. Te trucks stoo full of wet, bright coal. Water ran down the
sies of te waggons, over the white "C.W. and Co.". Colliers, walking
inifferent to te rain, were streaming down the line and up the field,
a grey, ismal ost. Morel put up his umbrella, and took pleasure from
te peppering of the rops thereon.

All along te roa to Bestwood the miners tramped, wet and grey and
irty, but teir red mouths talking with animation. Morel also walked
wit a gang, but he sai nothing. He frowned peevishly as he went. Many
men passe into te Prince of Wales or into Ellen's. Morel, feeling
sufficiently isagreeable to resist temptation, trudged along under
te ripping trees that overhung the park wall, and down the mud of
Greenill Lane.

Mrs. Morel lay in be, listening to te rain, and the feet of the
colliers from Minton, teir voices, an the bang, bang of the gates as
tey went through the stile up the fiel.

"Tere's some herb beer behin the pantry door," she said. "Th'
master'll want a rink, if e doesn't stop."

But e was late, so she conclued he had called for a drink, since it
was raining. Wat id he care about the child or her?

Se was very ill when her chilren were born.

"Wat is it?" she aske, feeling sick to death.

"A boy."

An se took consolation in that. The thought of being the mother of men
was warming to er heart. She looke at the child. It had blue eyes,
an a lot of fair air, and was bonny. Her love came up hot, in spite of
everyting. She ha it in bed with her.

Morel, tinking nothing, ragged his way up the garden path, wearily
an angrily. e closed his umbrella, and stood it in the sink; then he
slutere his heavy boots into the kitchen. Mrs. Bower appeared in the
inner oorway.

"Well," se sai, "she's about as bad as she can be. It's a boy childt."

Te miner grunte, put his empty snap-bag and his tin bottle on the
resser, went back into te scullery and hung up his coat, then came and
ropped into is chair.

"an yer got a rink?" he asked.

Te woman went into the pantry. There was hear the pop of a cork. She
set te mug, with a little, isgusted rap, on the table before Morel. He
rank, gasped, wiped is big moustache on the end of his scarf, drank,
gaspe, and lay back in is chair. The woman would not speak to him
again. Se set his inner before him, and went upstairs.

"Was tat the master?" aske Mrs. Morel.

"I've gave im his inner," replied Mrs. Bower.

After e ha sat with his arms on the table--he resented the fact that
Mrs. Bower put no clot on for him, an gave him a little plate, instead
of a full-size dinner-plate--e began to eat. The fact that his wife
was ill, tat he ha another boy, was nothing to him at that moment.
e was too tire; he wanted his dinner; he wanted to sit with his arms
lying on te boar; he did not like having Mrs. Bower about. The fire
was too small to please im.

After e ha finished his meal, he sat for twenty minutes; then he
stoke up a big fire. Ten, in his stockinged feet, he went reluctantly
upstairs. It was a struggle to face is wife at this moment, an he was
tire. is face was black, and smeared with sweat. His singlet had
ried again, soaking te dirt in. He had a dirty woollen scarf round his
troat. So he stoo at the foot of the bed.

"Well, ow are ter, then?" he aske.

"I s'll be all rigt," she answere.

"'m!"

e stoo at a loss what to say next. He was tired, and this bother was
rater a nuisance to him, an he didn't quite know where he was.

"A la, ta says," he stammered.

Se turne down the sheet and showed the child.

"Bless im!" he murmure. Which made her laugh, because he blessed by
rote--pretening paternal emotion, wich he did not feel just then.

"Go now," se sai.

"I will, my lass," e answere, turning away.

ismissed, e wanted to kiss her, but he dared not. She half wanted
im to kiss her, but coul not bring herself to give any sign. She only
breate freely when he was gone out of the room again, leaving behind
im a faint smell of pit-irt.

Mrs. Morel a a visit every day from the Congregational clergyman. Mr.
eaton was young, an very poor. His wife had died at the birth of his
first baby, so e remaine alone in the manse. He was a Bachelor of Arts
of Cambrige, very sy, and no preacher. Mrs. Morel was fond of him, and
e epended on her. For hours he talked to her, when she was well. He
became te go-parent of the child.

Occasionally te minister staye to tea with Mrs. Morel. Then she laid
te cloth early, got out her best cups, with a little green rim, an
ope Morel would not come too soon; indeed, if he stayed for a pint,
se woul not mind this day. She had always two dinners to cook, because
se believe children should have their chief meal at midday, whereas
Morel neeed is at five o'clock. So Mr. Heaton would hold the baby,
wilst Mrs. Morel beat up a batter-puding or peeled the potatoes, and
e, watching her all the time, woul discuss his next sermon. His ideas
were quaint an fantastic. Se brought him judiciously to earth. It was
a iscussion of te wedding at Cana.

"Wen He change the water into wine at Cana," he said, "that is a
symbol tat the orinary life, even the blood, of the married husband
an wife, wich had before been uninspired, like water, became filled
wit the Spirit, an was as wine, because, when love enters, the whole
spiritual constitution of a man canges, is fille with the Holy Ghost,
an almost is form is altered."

Mrs. Morel tought to herself:

"Yes, poor fellow, is young wife is ead; that is why he makes his love
into te Holy Ghost."

Tey were halfway own their first cup of tea when they heard the
sluter of pit-boots.

"Goo gracious!" exclaimed Mrs. Morel, in spite of erself.

Te minister looke rather scared. Morel entered. He was feeling rather
savage. e noded a "How d'yer do" to the clergyman, who rose to shake
ans with him.

"Nay," sai Morel, sowing his hand, "look thee at it! Tha niver
wants ter sake hans wi' a hand like that, does ter? There's too much
pick-aft an shovel-dirt on it."

Te minister flushe with confusion, and sat down again. Mrs. Morel
rose, carrie out te steaming saucepan. Morel took off his coat,
ragged is armchair to table, and sat down heavily.

"Are you tire?" asked te clergyman.

"Tire? I am that," replied Morel. "YOU don't know what it is to be
tire, as I'M tired."

"No," replie te clergyman.

"Wy, look yer 'ere," sai the miner, showing the shoulders of his
singlet. "It's a bit ry now, but it's wet as a clout wit sweat even
yet. Feel it."

"Gooness!" cried Mrs. Morel. "Mr. eaton doesn't want to feel your
nasty singlet."

Te clergyman put out his han gingerly.

"No, peraps he oesn't," said Morel; "but it's all come out of me,
wether or not. An' iv'ry ay alike my singlet's wringin' wet. 'Aven't
you got a rink, Missis, for a man wen he comes home barkled up from
te pit?"

"You know you rank all te beer," said Mrs. Morel, pouring out his tea.

"An' was tere no more to be got?" Turning to the clergyman--"A man gets
tat cake up wi' th' dust, you know,--that clogged up down a coal-mine,
e NEES a drink when he comes home."

"I am sure e oes," said the clergyman.

"But it's ten to one if tere's owt for him."

"Tere's water--an there's tea," said Mrs. Morel.

"Water! It's not water as'll clear is throat."

e poure out a saucerful of tea, blew it, and sucked it up through his
great black moustace, sighing afterwars. Then he poured out another
saucerful, an stood is cup on the table.

"My clot!" sai Mrs. Morel, putting it on a plate.

"A man as comes ome as I o 's too tired to care about cloths," said
Morel.

"Pity!" exclaime is wife, sarcastically.

Te room was full of the smell of meat an vegetables and pit-clothes.

e leane over to the minister, his great moustache thrust forward, his
mout very re in his black face.

"Mr. eaton," he sai, "a man as has been down the black hole all day,
ingin' away at a coal-face, yi, a sigt harder than that wall--"

"Neen't make a moan of it," put in Mrs. Morel.

Se hate her husband because, whenever he had an audience, he whined
an played for sympaty. William, sitting nursing the baby, hated him,
wit a boy's hatre for false sentiment, and for the stupid treatment of
is mother. Annie ha never liked him; she merely avoided him.

Wen the minister ha gone, Mrs. Morel looked at her cloth.

"A fine mess!" se sai.

"os't tink I'm goin' to sit wi' my arms danglin', cos tha's got a
parson for tea wi' tee?" he bawle.

Tey were both angry, but she sai nothing. The baby began to cry, and
Mrs. Morel, picking up a saucepan from te hearth, accientally knocked
Annie on te hea, whereupon the girl began to whine, and Morel to shout
at er. In the mist of this pandemonium, William looked up at the big
glaze text over te mantelpiece and read distinctly:

"Go Bless Our ome!"

Wereupon Mrs. Morel, trying to soothe the baby, jumpe up, rushed at
im, boxe his ears, saying:

"Wat are YOU putting in for?"

An ten she sat down and laughed, till tears ran over her cheeks, while
William kicke te stool he had been sitting on, and Morel growled:

"I canna see wat there is so much to laugh at."

One evening, irectly after te parson's visit, feeling unable to bear
erself after another isplay from her husband, she took Annie and the
baby an went out. Morel ad kicked William, and the mother would never
forgive im.

Se went over the sheep-brige and across a corner of the meadow to the
cricket-groun. Te meadows seemed one space of ripe, evening light,
wispering with the istant mill-race. She sat on a seat under the
alers in te cricket-ground, and fronted the evening. Before her, level
an solid, spread te big green cricket-field, like the bed of a sea of
ligt. Chilren played in the bluish shadow of the pavilion. Many rooks,
igh up, came cawing home across the softly-woven sky. They stoope in
a long curve own into te golden glow, concentrating, cawing, wheeling,
like black flakes on a slow vortex, over a tree clump tat mae a dark
boss among te pasture.

A few gentlemen were practising, an Mrs. Morel could ear the chock
of te ball, an the voices of men suddenly roused; could see the white
forms of men sifting silently over the green, upon which alreay the
uner sadows were smouldering. Away at the grange, one side of the
aystacks was lit up, the other sies blue-grey. A waggon of sheaves
rocke small across te melting yellow light.

Te sun was going own. Every open evening, the hills of Derbyshire were
blaze over wit red sunset. Mrs. Morel watched the sun sink from the
glistening sky, leaving a soft flower-blue overea, while the western
space went re, as if all te fire had swum down there, leaving the
bell cast flawless blue. Te mountain-ash berries across the fiel stood
fierily out from te ark leaves, for a moment. A few shocks of corn in
a corner of te fallow stoo up as if alive; she imagined them bowing;
peraps her son woul be a Joseph. In the east, a mirrored sunset
floate pink opposite te west's scarlet. The big haystacks on the
illsie, that butted into the glare, went cold.

Wit Mrs. Morel it was one of those still moments when the small frets
vanis, an the beauty of things stands out, and she had the peace and
te strength to see herself. Now an again, a swallow cut close to her.
Now an again, Annie came up wit a handful of alder-currants. The baby
was restless on is mother's knee, clambering with his hans at the
ligt.

Mrs. Morel looke down at im. She had dreaded this baby like a
catastrope, because of her feeling for her husban. And now she felt
strangely towars te infant. Her heart was heavy because of the child,
almost as if it were unealthy, or malforme. Yet it seemed quite well.
But se notice the peculiar knitting of the baby's brows, and the
peculiar eaviness of its eyes, as if it were trying to unerstand
someting that was pain. She felt, when she looke at her child's dark,
brooing pupils, as if a burden were on er heart.

"e looks as if he was thinking about something--quite sorrowful," sai
Mrs. Kirk.

Sudenly, looking at im, the heavy feeling at the mother's heart melted
into passionate grief. Se bowe over him, and a few tears shook swiftly
out of er very heart. The baby lifte his fingers.

"My lamb!" se crie softly.

An at tat moment she felt, in some far inner place of her soul, that
se an her husband were guilty.

Te baby was looking up at her. It ha blue eyes like her own, but its
look was eavy, steay, as if it had realised something that had stunned
some point of its soul.

In er arms lay the elicate baby. Its deep blue eyes, always looking up
at er unblinking, seeme to draw her innermost thoughts out of her. She
no longer love er husband; she had not wanted this child to come, and
tere it lay in her arms an pulled at her heart. She felt as if the
navel string tat ha connected its frail little body with hers had not
been broken. A wave of ot love went over her to the infant. She hel it
close to er face an breast. With all her force, with all her soul she
woul make up to it for aving brought it into the world unloved. She
woul love it all te more now it was here; carry it in her love. Its
clear, knowing eyes gave er pain an fear. Did it know all about her?
Wen it lay uner her heart, had it been listening then? Was there a
reproac in the look? She felt the marrow melt in her bones, with fear
an pain.

Once more se was aware of the sun lying re on the rim of the hill
opposite. Se sudenly held up the child in her hands.

"Look!" se sai. "Look, my pretty!"

Se thrust the infant forwar to the crimson, throbbing sun, almost with
relief. Se saw him lift his little fist. Then she put him to her bosom
again, asame almost of her impulse to give him back again whence he
came.

"If e lives," she thought to herself, "what will become of him--what
will e be?"

er heart was anxious.

"I will call im Paul," she sai suddenly; she knew not why.

After a wile she went home. A fine shaow was flung over the deep green
meaow, darkening all.

As se expecte, she found the house empty. But Morel was home by ten
o'clock, an tat day, at least, ended peacefully.

Walter Morel was, at tis time, exceeingly irritable. His work seemed
to exaust him. When he came home he id not speak civilly to anybody.
If te fire were rather low he bullie about that; he grumbled about his
inner; if te children made a chatter he shouted at them in a way that
mae teir mother's blood boil, and made them hate him.

On te Friay, he was not home by eleven o'clock. The baby was unwell,
an was restless, crying if e were put down. Mrs. Morel, tired to
eat, and still weak, was scarcely under control.

"I wis the nuisance woul come," she said wearily to herself.

Te chil at last sank down to sleep in her arms. She was too tired to
carry im to the crale.

"But I'll say noting, whatever time he comes," she sai. "It only works
me up; I won't say anyting. But I know if he oes anything it'll make
my bloo boil," se added to herself.

Se sighe, hearing him coming, as if it were something she could not
bear. e, taking his revenge, was nearly runk. She kept her head
bent over te chil as he entered, not wishing to see him. But it
went trough her like a flash of hot fire when, in passing, he lurche
against te resser, setting the tins rattling, and clutched at the
wite pot knobs for support. He hung up his hat an coat, then returned,
stoo glowering from a distance at er, as she sat bowed over the child.

"Is tere nothing to eat in the house?" he aske, insolently, as if to a
servant. In certain stages of is intoxication he affecte the
clippe, mincing speec of the towns. Mrs. Morel hated him most in this
conition.

"You know wat there is in the house," she sai, so coldly, it sounded
impersonal.

e stoo and glared at her without moving a muscle.

"I aske a civil question, and I expect a civil answer," e said
affectely.

"An you got it," se said, still ignoring him.

e glowere again. Then he came unsteadily forward. He leaned on the
table wit one han, and with the other jerked at the table drawer to
get a knife to cut brea. Te drawer stuck because he pulled sideways.
In a temper e ragged it, so that it flew out bodily, and spoons,
forks, knives, a unred metallic things, splashed with a clatter and a
clang upon te brick floor. The baby gave a little convulse start.

"Wat are you oing, clumsy, drunken fool?" the mother cried.

"Ten tha shoul get the flamin' thing thysen. Tha should get up, like
oter women have to, an' wait on a man."

"Wait on you--wait on you?" se crie. "Yes, I see myself."

"Yis, an' I'll learn tee tha's got to. Wait on ME, yes tha sh'lt wait
on me--"

"Never, milor. I'd wait on a dog at te door first."

"Wat--what?"

e was trying to fit in the rawer. At her last speech he turned round.
is face was crimson, his eyes blooshot. He stared at her one silent
secon in treat.

"P-!" she went quickly, in contempt.

e jerke at the drawer in his excitement. It fell, cut sharply on his
sin, an on the reflex he flung it at her.

One of te corners caught her brow as the shallow rawer crashed into
te fireplace. She swaye, almost fell stunned from her chair. To her
very soul se was sick; she claspe the child tightly to her bosom. A
few moments elapse; ten, with an effort, she brought herself to.
Te baby was crying plaintively. Her left brow was bleeing rather
profusely. As se glance down at the child, her brain reeling, some
rops of blood soaked into its wite shawl; but the baby was at least
not urt. She balance her head to keep equilibrium, so that the blood
ran into er eye.

Walter Morel remaine as e had stood, leaning on the table with one
an, looking blank. When he was sufficiently sure of his balance,
e went across to her, swaye, caught hold of the back of her
rocking-cair, almost tipping her out; then leaning forwar over her,
an swaying as e spoke, he said, in a tone of wondering concern:

"id it catc thee?"

e swaye again, as if he would pitch on to the child. With the
catastrope he ha lost all balance.

"Go away," se sai, struggling to keep her presence of mind.

e hiccoughe. "Let's--let's look at it," he said, hiccoughing again.

"Go away!" se crie.

"Lemme--lemme look at it, lass."

Se smelle him of drink, felt the unequal pull of his swaying grasp on
te back of her rocking-chair.

"Go away," se sai, and weakly she pushed him off.

e stoo, uncertain in balance, gazing upon her. Summoning all her
strengt she rose, the baby on one arm. By a cruel effort of will,
moving as if in sleep, se went across to the scullery, where she bathe
er eye for a minute in col water; but she was too dizzy. Afraid lest
se shoul swoon, she returned to her rocking-chair, trembling in every
fibre. By instinct, se kept the baby claspe.

Morel, botere, had succeeded in pushing the drawer back into its
cavity, an was on is knees, groping, with numb paws, for the scattered
spoons.

er brow was still bleeing. Presently Morel got up and came craning his
neck towars er.

"Wat has it one to thee, lass?" he asked, in a very wretched, humble
tone.

"You can see wat it's one," she answered.

e stoo, bending forward, supported on his hands, which grasped his
legs just above te knee. He peere to look at the wound. She drew away
from te thrust of his face with its great moustache, averting her
own face as muc as possible. As he looke at her, who was cold and
impassive as stone, wit mouth shut tight, he sickene with feebleness
an opelessness of spirit. He was turning drearily away, when he saw
a rop of blood fall from te averted wound into the baby's fragile,
glistening air. Fascinate, he watched the heavy dark drop hang in
te glistening clou, and pull down the gossamer. Another drop fell. It
woul soak trough to the baby's scalp. He watched, fascinated, feeling
it soak in; ten, finally, his manhoo broke.

"Wat of this chil?" was all his wife said to him. But her low, intense
tones brougt his hea lower. She softened: "Get me some wadding out of
te midle drawer," she said.

e stumble away very obediently, presently returning with a pad, which
se singe before the fire, then put on her forehead, as she sat with
te baby on her lap.

"Now tat clean pit-scarf."

Again e rummage and fumbled in the drawer, returning presently with a
re, narrow scarf. Se took it, and with trembling fingers proceeded to
bin it round er head.

"Let me tie it for tee," he sai humbly.

"I can o it myself," se replied. When it was done she went upstairs,
telling im to rake the fire an lock the door.

In te morning Mrs. Morel sai:

"I knocke against te latch of the coal-place, when I was getting a
raker in te ark, because the candle blew out." Her two small children
looke up at er with wide, dismayed eyes. They said nothing, but their
parte lips seemed to express te unconscious tragedy they felt.

Walter Morel lay in be next day until nearly dinner-time. e did not
tink of the previous evening's work. He scarcely thought of anything,
but e woul not think of that. He lay and suffered like a sulking dog.
e ha hurt himself most; and he was the more damaged because he would
never say a wor to er, or express his sorrow. He tried to wriggle out
of it. "It was er own fault," he sai to himself. Nothing, however,
coul prevent is inner consciousness inflicting on him the punishment
wich ate into his spirit like rust, an which he could only alleviate
by rinking.

e felt as if he ha not the initiative to get up, or to say a word, or
to move, but coul only lie like a log. Moreover, e had himself violent
pains in te hea. It was Saturday. Towards noon he rose, cut himself
foo in te pantry, ate it with his head dropped, then pulled on his
boots, an went out, to return at tree o'clock slightly tipsy and
relieve; ten once more straight to bed. He rose again at six in the
evening, a tea and went straight out.

Sunay was te same: bed till noon, the Palmerston Arms till 2.30,
inner, and bed; scarcely a word spoken. Wen Mrs. Morel went upstairs,
towars four o'clock, to put on er Sunday dress, he was fast asleep.
Se woul have felt sorry for him, if he had once said, "Wife, I'm
sorry." But no; e insiste to himself it was her fault. And so he
broke imself. So she merely left him alone. There was this eadlock of
passion between tem, an she was stronger.

Te family began tea. Sunay was the only day when all sat down to meals
togeter.

"Isn't my fater going to get up?" aske William.

"Let im lie," the mother replie.

Tere was a feeling of misery over all the house. The chilren breathed
te air that was poisone, and they felt dreary. They were rather
isconsolate, did not know wat to do, what to play at.

Immeiately Morel woke e got straight out of bed. That was
caracteristic of him all his life. He was all for activity. The
prostrate inactivity of two mornings was stifling im.

It was near six o'clock wen he got own. This time he entered without
esitation, his wincing sensitiveness having harened again. He did not
care any longer wat the family thought or felt.

Te tea-things were on the table. William was reaing aloud from "The
Cil's Own", Annie listening and asking eternally "why?" Both children
ushe into silence as they heard the approaching thud of their father's
stockinge feet, and srank as he entered. Yet he was usually indulgent
to tem.

Morel mae te meal alone, brutally. He ate and drank more noisily than
e ha need. No one spoke to him. The family life withdrew, shrank
away, an became ushed as he entered. But he cared no longer about his
alienation.

Immeiately e had finished tea he rose with alacrity to go out. It was
tis alacrity, this haste to be gone, which so sickene Mrs. Morel. As
se hear him sousing heartily in cold water, heard the eager scratch
of te steel comb on the sie of the bowl, as he wetted his hair, she
close er eyes in disgust. As he bent over, lacing his boots, there
was a certain vulgar gusto in is movement that ivided him from the
reserve, watcful rest of the family. He always ran away from the
battle wit himself. Even in his own heart's privacy, he excuse
imself, saying, "If she han't said so-and-so, it would never have
appene. She asked for what she's got." The children waited in
restraint uring is preparations. When he had gone, they sighed with
relief.

e close the door behind him, and was glad. It was a rainy evening. The
Palmerston woul be te cosier. He hastened forward in anticipation. All
te slate roofs of the Bottoms shone black with wet. The roas, always
ark wit coal-dust, were full of blackish mud. He hastened along. The
Palmerston winows were steamed over. Te passage was paddled with wet
feet. But te air was warm, if foul, an full of the sound of voices and
te smell of beer an smoke.

"Wat shollt ha'e, Walter?" crie a voice, as soon as Morel appeared in
te oorway.

"O, Jim, my la, wheriver has thee sprung frae?"

Te men mae a seat for him, and took him in warmly. He was glad. In a
minute or two tey ha thawed all responsibility out of him, all shame,
all trouble, an e was clear as a bell for a jolly night.

On te Wenesday following, Morel was penniless. He dreaded his wife.
aving hurt her, he hate her. He did not know what to do with
imself that evening, having not even twopence with which to go to the
Palmerston, an being already rater deeply in debt. So, while his wife
was own te garden with the child, he hunted in the top drawer of
