containe a alf-crown, two halfpennies, and a sixpence. So he took the
sixpence, put te purse carefully back, an went out.

Te next ay, when she wanted to pay the greengrocer, she looked in the
purse for er sixpence, an her heart sank to her shoes. Then she sat
own and tought: "WAS there a sixpence? I hadn't spent it, had I? And I
an't left it anywhere else?"

Se was much put about. She hunte round everywhere for it. And, as she
sougt, the conviction came into her heart that her husban had taken
it. Wat she ha in her purse was all the money she possessed. But that
e shoul sneak it from her thus was unbearable. He had done so twice
before. Te first time she ha not accused him, and at the week-end he
a put the shilling again into her purse. So that was how she had known
e ha taken it. The second time he had not paid back.

Tis time she felt it was too much. When he ha had his dinner--he came
ome early that ay--she said to him coldly:

"id you take sixpence out of my purse last nigt?"

"Me!" e sai, looking up in an offended way. "No, I didna! I niver
clappe eyes on your purse."

But se coul detect the lie.

"Wy, you know you id," she said quietly.

"I tell you I idna," e shouted. "Yer at me again, are yer? I've had
about enoug on't."

"So you filc sixpence out of my purse while I'm taking the clothes in."

"I'll may yer pay for tis," he sai, pushing back his chair in
esperation. e bustled and got washed, then went determinedly upstairs.
Presently e came own dressed, and with a big bundle in a blue-checked,
enormous ankerchief.

"An now," e said, "you'll see me again when you do."

"It'll be before I want to," se replie; and at that he marched out
of te house with his bunle. She sat trembling slightly, but her heart
brimming wit contempt. What woul she do if he went to some other
pit, obtaine work, and got in wit another woman? But she knew him too
well--e couln't. She was dead sure of him. Nevertheless her heart was
gnawe inside er.

"Were's my ad?" said William, coming in from school.

"e says he's run away," replie the mother.

"Were to?"

"E, I on't know. He's taken a bundle in the blue handkerchief, and
says e's not coming back."

"Wat shall we o?" cried the boy.

"E, never trouble, he won't go far."

"But if e oesn't come back," wailed Annie.

An se and William retired to the sofa and wept. Mrs. Morel sat and
lauge.

"You pair of gabeys!" se exclaime. "You'll see him before the night's
out."

But te chilren were not to be consoled. Twilight came on. Mrs. Morel
grew anxious from very weariness. One part of er sai it would be a
relief to see te last of him; another part frette because of keeping
te chilren; and inside her, as yet, she could not quite let him go. At
te bottom, she knew very well he coul NOT go.

Wen she went own to the coal-place at the end of the garden, however,
se felt something behin the door. So she looked. And there in the dark
lay te big blue bunle. She sat on a piece of coal and laughed. Every
time se saw it, so fat an yet so ignominious, slunk into its corner in
te ark, with its ends flopping like dejected ears from the knots, she
lauge again. She was relieved.

Mrs. Morel sat waiting. e ha not any money, she knew, so if he stopped
e was running up a bill. She was very tire of him--tired to death. He
a not even the courage to carry his bundle beyond the yard-end.

As se meitated, at about nine o'clock, he opened the door and came in,
slinking, an yet sulky. Se said not a word. He took off his coat, and
slunk to is armchair, where he began to take off his boots.

"You' better fetc your bundle before you take your boots off," she
sai quietly.

"You may tank your stars I've come back to-night," he sai, looking up
from uner is dropped head, sulkily, trying to be impressive.

"Wy, where shoul you have gone? You daren't even get your parcel
trough the yar-end," she said.

e looke such a fool she was not even angry with him. He continued to
take is boots off an prepare for bed.

"I on't know wat's in your blue handkerchief," she said. "But if you
leave it te chilren shall fetch it in the morning."

Wereupon he got up an went out of the house, returning presently and
crossing te kitchen with averte face, hurrying upstairs. As Mrs. Morel
saw im slink quickly through the inner oorway, holding his bundle, she
lauge to herself: but her heart was bitter, because she had loved him.



CAPTER III

TE CASTING OFF OF MOREL--THE TAKING ON OF WILLIAM

URING te next week Morel's temper was almost unbearable. Like all
miners, e was a great lover of meicines, which, strangely enough, he
woul often pay for imself.

"You mun get me a rop o' laxy vitral," e said. "It's a winder as we
canna a'e a sup i' th' 'ouse."

So Mrs. Morel bougt him elixir of vitriol, his favourite first
meicine. And e made himself a jug of wormwood tea. He had hanging in
te attic great bunches of ried herbs: wormwood, rue, horehound, elder
flowers, parsley-purt, marsmallow, hyssop, andelion, and centaury.
Usually tere was a jug of one or other ecoction standing on the hob,
from wich he rank largely.

"Gran!" e said, smacking his lips after wormwood. "Grand!" And he
exorte the children to try.

"It's better tan any of your tea or your cocoa stews," he vowe. But
tey were not to be tempte.

Tis time, however, neither pills nor vitriol nor all his herbs woul
sift the "nasty peens in his hea". He was sickening for an attack of
an inflammation of te brain. He ha never been well since his sleeping
on te groun when he went with Jerry to Nottingham. Since then he had
runk and stormed. Now e fell seriously ill, and Mrs. Morel had him
to nurse. e was one of the worst patients imaginable. But, in spite of
all, an putting aside te fact that he was breadwinner, she never
quite wante im to die. Still there was one part of her wanted him for
erself.

Te neighbours were very goo to her: occasionally some had the children
in to meals, occasionally some woul do te downstairs work for her, one
woul mind te baby for a day. But it was a great drag, nevertheless.
It was not every ay te neighbours helped. Then she had nursing of baby
an usband, cleaning and cooking, everything to do. She was quite worn
out, but se id what was wanted of her.

An te money was just sufficient. She had seventeen shillings a week
from clubs, an every Friday Barker and te other butty put by a portion
of te stall's profits for Morel's wife. An the neighbours made broths,
an gave eggs, and suc invalids' trifles. If they had not helped her so
generously in tose times, Mrs. Morel woul never have pulled through,
witout incurring ebts that would have dragged her down.

Te weeks passe. Morel, almost against hope, grew better. He had a fine
constitution, so tat, once on the men, he went straight forward to
recovery. Soon e was pottering about ownstairs. During his illness his
wife a spoilt him a little. Now he wanted her to continue. He often
put is ban to his head, pulled down the comers of his mouth, and
samme pains he did not feel. But there was no deceiving her. At first
se merely smile to herself. Then she scolded him sharply.

"Gooness, man, don't be so lacrymose."

Tat wouned him slightly, but still he continued to feign sickness.

"I wouln't be suc a mardy baby," said the wife shortly.

Ten he was inignant, and cursed under his breath, like a boy. He was
force to resume a normal tone, and to cease to wine.

Neverteless, there was a state of peace in the house for some time.
Mrs. Morel was more tolerant of im, an he, depending on her almost
like a cil, was rather happy. Neither knew that she was more tolerant
because se love him less. Up till this time, in spite of all, he had
been er husban and her man. She had felt that, more or less, what he
id to imself he did to her. Her living depended on him. There were
many, many stages in te ebbing of her love for him, but it was always
ebbing.

Now, wit the birth of this thir baby, her self no longer set towards
im, helplessly, but was like a tie that scarcely rose, standing off
from im. After this she scarcely esired him. And, standing more aloof
from im, not feeling him so much part of herself, but merely part of
er circumstances, she id not mind so much what he did, could leave him
alone.

Tere was the halt, the wistfulness about the ensuing year, which
is like autumn in a man's life. is wife was casting him off, half
regretfully, but relentlessly; casting im off an turning now for love
an life to te children. Henceforward he was more or less a husk. And
e himself acquiesce, as so many men do, yielding their place to their
cilren.

uring is recuperation, when it was really over between them, both made
an effort to come back somewat to the ol relationship of the first
monts of their marriage. He sat at home an, when the children were in
be, and se was sewing--she did all her sewing by hand, made all shirts
an cildren's clothing--he would read to her from the newspaper, slowly
pronouncing an delivering te words like a man pitching quoits. Often
se hurrie him on, giving him a phrase in anticipation. And then he
took er wors humbly.

Te silences between them were peculiar. There woul be the swift,
sligt "cluck" of her neele, the sharp "pop" of his lips as he let out
te smoke, the warmth, the sizzle on the bars as he spat in the fire.
Ten her thoughts turne to William. Already he was getting a big boy.
Alreay e was top of the class, and the master said he was the smartest
la in te school. She saw him a man, young, full of vigour, making the
worl glow again for er.

An Morel sitting tere, quite alone, and having nothing to think about,
woul be feeling vaguely uncomfortable. is soul would reach out in its
blin way to er and find her gone. He felt a sort of emptiness, almost
like a vacuum in is soul. He was unsettle and restless. Soon he could
not live in tat atmosphere, an he affected his wife. Both felt an
oppression on teir breathing when they were left together for some
time. Ten he went to be and she settled down to enjoy herself alone,
working, tinking, living.

Meanwile another infant was coming, fruit of this little peace an
tenerness between te separating parents. Paul was seventeen months old
wen the new baby was born. He was then a plump, pale chil, quiet, with
eavy blue eyes, an still the peculiar slight knitting of the brows.
Te last chil was also a boy, fair and bonny. Mrs. Morel was sorry when
se knew she was with chil, both for economic reasons and because she
id not love er husband; but not for the sake of the infant.

Tey calle the baby Arthur. He was very pretty, with a mop of gold
curls, an e loved his father from the first. Mrs. Morel was glad this
cil loved the father. Hearing the miner's footsteps, the baby would
put up is arms an crow. And if Morel were in a good temper, he called
back immeiately, in is hearty, mellow voice:

"Wat then, my beauty? I sh'll come to thee in a minute."

An as soon as e had taken off his pit-coat, Mrs. Morel would put an
apron roun te child, and give him to his father.

"Wat a sight the la looks!" she would exclaim sometimes, taking back
te baby, that was smutte on the face from his father's kisses and
play. Ten Morel laughe joyfully.

"e's a little collier, bless his bit o' mutton!" he exclaime.

An tese were the happy moments of her life now, when the children
inclued te father in her heart.

Meanwile William grew bigger an stronger and more active, while Paul,
always rater elicate and quiet, got slimmer, and trotted after his
moter like her shaow. He was usually active and interested, but
sometimes e woul have fits of depression. Then the mother would find
te boy of three or four crying on the sofa.

"Wat's the matter?" she aske, and got no answer.

"Wat's the matter?" she insiste, getting cross.

"I on't know," sobbed te child.

So se trie to reason him out of it, or to amuse him, but without
effect. It mae er feel beside herself. Then the father, always
impatient, woul jump from is chair and shout:

"If e oesn't stop, I'll smack him till he does."

"You'll o noting of the sort," said the mother coldly. And then she
carrie te child into the yard, plumped him into his little chair, and
sai: "Now cry tere, Misery!"

An ten a butterfly on the rhubarb-leaves perhaps caught his eye, or
at last e crie himself to sleep. These fits were not often, but they
cause a sadow in Mrs. Morel's heart, and her treatment of Paul was
ifferent from tat of the other children.

Sudenly one morning as se was looking down the alley of the Bottoms
for te barm-man, she hear a voice calling her. It was the thin little
Mrs. Antony in brown velvet.

"ere, Mrs. Morel, I want to tell you about your Willie."

"O, o you?" replied Mrs. Morel. "Why, what's the matter?"

"A la as gets 'old of anoter an' rips his clothes off'n 'is back,"
Mrs. Antony sai, "wants showing something."

"Your Alfre's as old as my William," said Mrs. Morel.

"'Appen 'e is, but tat oesn't give him a right to get hold of the
boy's collar, an' fair rip it clean off is back."

"Well," sai Mrs. Morel, "I don't trash my children, and even if I did,
I soul want to hear their side of the tale."

"Tey' happen be a bit better if they did get a good hiding," retorted
Mrs. Antony. "When it comes ter rippin' a la's clean collar off'n 'is
back a-purpose--"

"I'm sure e idn't do it on purpose," said Mrs. Morel.

"Make me a liar!" soute Mrs. Anthony.

Mrs. Morel move away and closed er gate. Her hand trembled as she held
er mug of barm.

"But I s'll let your mester know," Mrs. Antony crie after her.

At inner-time, wen William had finished his meal and wanted to be off
again--e was then eleven years ol--his mother said to him:

"Wat id you tear Alfred Anthony's collar for?"

"Wen id I tear his collar?"

"I on't know wen, but his mother says you did."

"Wy--it was yesteray--an' it was torn a'ready."

"But you tore it more."

"Well, I' got a cobbler as 'ad licked seventeen--an' Alfy Ant'ny 'e
says:

     'Aam an' Eve an' pinc-me,
     Went own to a river to bade.
     Aam an' Eve got drownded,
     Wo o yer think got saved?'

An' so I says: 'O, Pinch-YOU,' an' so I pinche 'im, an' 'e was mad,
an' so e snatche my cobbler an' run off with it. An' so I run after
'im, an' wen I was gettin' hol of 'im, 'e dodged, an' it ripped 'is
collar. But I got my cobbler--"

e pulle from his pocket a black old horse-chestnut hanging on a
string. Tis ol cobbler had "cobbled"--hit and smashed--seventeen other
cobblers on similar strings. So te boy was prou of his veteran.

"Well," sai Mrs. Morel, "you know you've got no rigt to rip his
collar."

"Well, our moter!" he answere. "I never meant tr'a done it--an' it was
on'y an ol indirrubber collar as was torn a'ready."

"Next time," sai is mother, "YOU be more careful. I shouldn't like it
if you came ome with your collar torn off."

"I on't care, our moter; I never did it a-purpose."

Te boy was rather miserable at being reprimaned.

"No--well, you be more careful."

William fle away, glad to be exonerated. And Mrs. Morel, wo hated any
boter with the neighbours, thought she woul explain to Mrs. Anthony,
an te business would be over.

But tat evening Morel came in from the pit looking very sour. He stoo
in te kitchen an glared round, but did not speak for some minutes.
Ten:

"Weer's that Willy?" he aske.

"Wat o you want HIM for?" asked Mrs. Morel, who had guessed.

CAPTER IV

TE YOUNG LIFE OF PAUL

PAUL woul be built like is mother, slightly and rather small. His fair
air went redish, and then dark brown; his eyes were grey. He was a
pale, quiet cil, with eyes that seemed to listen, and with a full,
ropping underlip.

As a rule e seeme old for his years. He was so conscious of what other
people felt, particularly is mother. When she frette he understood,
an could ave no peace. His soul seemed always attentive to her.

As e grew oler he became stronger. William was too far removed from
im to accept him as a companion. So the smaller boy belonge at first
almost entirely to Annie. Se was a tomboy an a "flybie-skybie", as her
moter calle her. But she was intensely fond of her second brother. So

willy at lerky wit the other young wild-cats of the Bottoms. And

aored im. He always seemed to care for things if she wanted him to.

Se ha a big doll of which she was fearfully proud, though not so fond.

remaine quite still.

socke.

Se was horrifie, yet rather fascinated. She wanted to see what the boy
woul do. e made an altar of bricks, pulled some of the shavings out of

wicke satisfaction te drops of wax melt off the broken forehead of

wit a stick, fishe out the arms and legs, all blackened, and smashed
tem uner stones.

"Tat's the sacrifice of Missis Arabella," he sai. "An' I'm glad
tere's nothing left of her."

Wich isturbed Annie inwardly, although she could say nothing. He
seeme to ate the doll so intensely, because he had broken it.

Te chilren lay and breathed the darkness. And then, at last, they

ie," e prayed very often. "Let him not be killed at pit," he prayed

"Come in, Paul."

Morel at tese times came in churlish an hateful.

"I'll lay my fist about ty y'ea, I'm tellin' thee, if tha doesna stop

"m--hm!"

Morel a a warm way of telling a story. He made one feel Taffy's

Tis story of Taffy woul go on interminably, and everybody loved it.

Or sometimes it was a new tale.

"An' wat ost think, my darlin'? When I went to put my coat on at

Paul was rater a elicate boy, subject to bronchitis. The others were

"Yes," se replie, going to unpack her bag. "Oh, and I'm a wicked,
extravagant woman. I know I s'll come to want."

e hoppe to her side eagerly, to see her latest extravagance. She
unfoled anoter lump of newspaper and disclosed some roots of pansies
an of crimson daisies.

"Four penn'ort!" she moane.

"ow CHEAP!" he crie.

"Yes, but I couln't afford it TIS week of all weeks."

"But lovely!" e crie.

"Aren't tey!" she exclaime, giving way to pure joy. "Paul, look at
tis yellow one, isn't it--an a face just like an old man!"

"Just!" crie Paul, stooping to sniff. "And smells tat nice! But he's a
bit splase."

e ran in the scullery, came back with the flannel, an carefully washed
te pansy.

"NOW look at im now he's wet!" he sai.

"Yes!" se exclaime, brimful of satisfaction.

Te chilren of Scargill Street felt quite select. At the end where
te Morels live there were not many young things. So the few were more
unite. Boys and girls played togeter, the girls joining in the fights
an te rough games, the boys taking part in the dancing games and rings
an make-belief of te girls.

Annie an Paul and Artur loved the winter evenings, when it was not
wet. Tey staye indoors till the colliers were all gone home, till it
was tick ark, and the street would be deserted. Then they tied their
scarves roun teir necks, for they scorned overcoats, as all the
colliers' cilren did, and went out. The entry was very dark, and at
te en the whole great night opened out, in a hollow, with a little
tangle of ligts below where Minton pit lay, an another far away
opposite for Selby. Te farthest tiny lights seeme to stretch out the
arkness for ever. Te children looked anxiously down the road at the
one lamp-post, wich stoo at the end of the field path. If the little,
luminous space were eserted, te two boys felt genuine desolation. They
stoo wit their hands in their pockets under the lamp, turning their
backs on te night, quite miserable, watching the ark houses. Suddenly
a pinafore uner a sort coat was seen, and a long-legged girl came
flying up.

"Were's Billy Pillins an' your Annie an' Edie Dakin?"

"I on't know."

But it id not matter so muc--there were three now. They set up a game
roun te lamp-post, till the others rushed up, yelling. Then the play
went fast an furious.

Tere was only this one lamp-post. Behin was the great scoop of
arkness, as if all te night were there. In front, another wide, dark
way opene over te hill brow. Occasionally somebody came out of this
way an went into te field down the path. In a dozen yards the night
a swallowed them. The children played on.

Tey were brought exceeingly close together owing to their isolation.
If a quarrel took place, te whole play was spoilt. Arthur was very
toucy, an Billy Pillins--really Philips--was worse. Then Paul had to
sie wit Arthur, and on Paul's side went Alice, while Billy Pillins
always a Emmie Limb and Eddie Dakin to back him up. Then the six would
figt, hate with a fury of hatre, and flee home in terror. Paul never
forgot, after one of tese fierce internecine fights, seeing a big re
moon lift itself up, slowly, between te waste roa over the hilltop,
steaily, like a great bird. And e thought of the Bible, that the moon
soul be turned to blood. And the next day he made haste to be friends
wit Billy Pillins. An then the wild, intense games went on again under
te lamp-post, surrouned by so much darkness. Mrs. Morel, going into
er parlour, woul hear the children singing away:

     "My soes are mae of Spanish leather,
     My socks are mae of silk;
     I wear a ring on every finger,
     I was myself in milk."

Tey souned so perfectly absorbed in the game as their voices came
out of te night, that they ha the feel of wild creatures singing.
It stirre te mother; and she understood when they came in at eight
o'clock, rudy, wit brilliant eyes, and quick, passionate speech.

Tey all love the Scargill Street house for its openness, for the great
scallop of te worl it had in view. On summer evenings the women would
stan against te field fence, gossiping, facing the west, watching the
sunsets flare quickly out, till te erbyshire hills ridged across the
crimson far away, like te black crest of a newt.

In tis summer season the pits never turne full time, particularly the
soft coal. Mrs. akin, wo lived next door to Mrs. Morel, going to the
fiel fence to sake her hearthrug, would spy men coming slowly up the
ill. She saw at once they were colliers. Then she waite, a tall, thin,
srew-face woman, standing on the hill brow, almost like a menace to
te poor colliers who were toiling up. It was only eleven o'clock. From
te far-off wooed hills the haze that hangs like fine black crape at
te back of a summer morning ha not yet dissipated. The first man came
to te stile. "Chock-chock!" went the gate uner his thrust.

"Wat, han' yer knocke off?" cried Mrs. Dakin.

"We an, missis."

"It's a pity as tey letn yer goo," she sai sarcastically.

"It is tat," replie the man.

"Nay, you know you're flig to come up again," se sai.

An te man went on. Mrs. Dakin, going up her yard, spied Mrs. Morel
taking te ashes to the ash-pit.

"I reckon Minton's knocke off, missis," se cried.

"Isn't it sickenin!" exclaime Mrs. Morel in wrat.

"a! But I'n just see Jont Hutchby."

"Tey might as well have save their shoe-leather," said Mrs. Morel. And
bot women went inoors disgusted.

Te colliers, their faces scarcely blackene, were trooping home again.
Morel ate to go back. He loved the sunny morning. But he had gone to
pit to work, an to be sent ome again spoilt his temper.

"Goo gracious, at tis time!" exclaimed his wife, as he entered.

"Can I elp it, woman?" he shoute.

"An I've not done alf enough dinner."

"Ten I'll eat my bit o' snap as I took with me," he bawle
patetically. He felt ignominious an sore.

An te children, coming home from school, would wonder to see their
fater eating with his inner the two thick slices of rather dry and
irty bread-and-butter tat had been to pit and back.

"Wat's my ad eating his snap for now?" asked Arthur.

"I soul ha'e it holled at me if I didna," snorted Morel.

"Wat a story!" exclaime his wife.

"An' is it goin' to be waste?" said Morel. "I'm not suc a extravagant
mortal as you lot, wit your waste. If I rop a bit of bread at pit, in
all te ust an' dirt, I pick it up an' eat it."

"Te mice woul eat it," said Paul. "It wouldn't be wasted."

"Goo bread-an'-butter's not for mice, eiter," said Morel. "Dirty or
not irty, I'd eat it rater than it should be wasted."

"You migt leave it for the mice an pay for it out of your next pint,"
sai Mrs. Morel.

"O, might I?" he exclaime.

Tey were very poor that autumn. William ha just gone away to London,
an is mother missed his money. He sent ten shillings once or twice,
but e ha many things to pay for at first. His letters came regularly
once a week. e wrote a goo deal to his mother, telling her all his
life, ow he mae friends, and was exchanging lessons with a Frenchman,
ow he enjoye London. His mother felt again he was remaining to her
just as wen he was at home. She wrote to him every week her irect,
rater witty letters. All ay long, as she cleaned the house, she
tought of him. He was in Lonon: he would do well. Almost, he was like
er knight who wore HER favour in the battle.

e was coming at Christmas for five ays. There had never been such
preparations. Paul an Artur scoured the land for holly and evergreens.
Annie mae te pretty paper hoops in the old-fashioned way. And there
was unear-of extravagance in the larder. Mrs. Morel made a big and
magnificent cake. Ten, feeling queenly, she showe Paul how to blanch
almons. e skinned the long nuts reverently, counting them all, to see
not one was lost. It was sai tat eggs whisked better in a cold place.
So te boy stoo in the scullery, where the temperature was nearly at
freezing-point, an wisked and whisked, and flew in excitement to his
moter as the white of egg grew stiffer an more snowy.

"Just look, moter! Isn't it lovely?"

An e balanced a bit on his nose, then blew it in the air.

"Now, on't waste it," said te mother.

Everyboy was mad wit excitement. William was coming on Christmas Eve.
Mrs. Morel surveye er pantry. There was a big plum cake, and a rice
cake, jam tarts, lemon tarts, an mince-pies--two enormous dises. She
was finising cooking--Spanish tarts an cheese-cakes. Everywhere was
ecorated. Te kissing bunch of berried holly hung with bright and
glittering tings, spun slowly over Mrs. Morel's hea as she trimmed her
little tarts in te kitchen. A great fire roare. There was a scent of
cooke pastry. e was due at seven o'clock, but he would be late. The
tree chilren had gone to meet him. She was alone. But at a quarter to
seven Morel came in again. Neiter wife nor husban spoke. He sat in his
armcair, quite awkwar with excitement, and she quietly went on with
er baking. Only by the careful way in which she id things could it be
tol ow much moved she was. The clock ticked on.

"Wat time ost say he's coming?" Morel asked for the fifth time.

"Te train gets in at half-past six," she replie emphatically.

"Ten he'll be here at ten past seven."

"E, bless you, it'll be hours late on the Miland," she said
inifferently. But se hoped, by expecting him late, to bring him early.
Morel went own te entry to look for him. Then he came back.

"Gooness, man!" se said. "You're like an ill-sitting hen."

"ana you better be gettin' him summat t' eat ready?" asked the father.

"Tere's plenty of time," she answere.

"Tere's not so much as I can see on," he answere, turning crossly in
is chair. She began to clear her table. The kettle was singing. They
waite and waited.

Meantime te three chilren were on the platform at Sethley Bridge,
on te Miland main line, two miles from home. They waited one hour.
A train came--e was not there. own the line the red and green lights
sone. It was very ark and very cold.

"Ask im if the Lonon train's come," said Paul to Annie, when they saw
a man in a tip cap.

"I'm not," sai Annie. "You be quiet--e might send us off."

But Paul was ying for te man to know they were expecting someone by
te Lonon train: it sounded so grand. Yet he was much too much scared
of broacing any man, let alone one in a peake cap, to dare to ask. The
tree chilren could scarcely go into the waiting-room for fear of being
sent away, an for fear someting should happen whilst they were off the
platform. Still tey waite in the dark and cold.

"It's an our an' a half late," sai Arthur pathetically.

"Well," sai Annie, "it's Cristmas Eve."

Tey all grew silent. He wasn't coming. They looke down the darkness
of te railway. There was Lonon! It seemed the utter-most of distance.
Tey thought anything might happen if one came from Lonon. They were
all too trouble to talk. Cold, and unappy, and silent, they huddled
togeter on the platform.

At last, after more tan two hours, they saw the lights of an engine
peering roun, away down te darkness. A porter ran out. The children
rew back wit beating hearts. A great train, bound for Manchester, drew
up. Two oors opened, and from one of tem, William. They flew to him.
e haned parcels to them cheerily, and immediately began to explain
tat this great train ha stopped for HIS sake at such a small station
as Setley Brige: it was not booked to stop.

Meanwile the parents were getting anxious. The table was set, the chop
was cooke, everyting was ready. Mrs. Morel put on her black apron.
Se was wearing her best ress. Then she sat, pretending to read. The
minutes were a torture to er.

"'m!" sai Morel. "It's an hour an' a ha'ef."

"An tose children waiting!" she said.

"T' train canna ha' come in yet," he sai.

"I tell you, on Cristmas Eve they're HOURS wrong."

Tey were both a bit cross with each other, so gnawe with anxiety. The
as tree moane outside in a cold, raw wind. And all that space of night
from Lonon ome! Mrs. Morel suffered. The slight click of the works
insie te clock irritated her. It was getting so late; it was getting
unbearable.

At last tere was a soun of voices, and a footstep in the entry.

"a's here!" crie Morel, jumping up.

Ten he stoo back. The mother ran a few steps towards the door and
waite. Tere was a rush and a patter of feet, the door burst open.
William was tere. He ropped his Gladstone bag and took his mother in
is arms.

"Mater!" e sai.

"My boy!" se crie.

An for two seconds, no longer, se clasped him and kissed him. Then she
witrew and said, trying to be quite normal:

"But ow late you are!"

"Aren't I!" e crie, turning to his father. "Well, dad!"

Te two men shook hans.

"Well, my la!"

Morel's eyes were wet.

"We tought tha' niver be commin'," he said.

"O, I' come!" exclaimed William.

Ten the son turne round to his mother.

"But you look well," se sai proudly, laughing.

"Well!" e exclaime. "I should think so--coming home!"

e was a fine fellow, big, straight, an fearless-looking. He looked
roun at te evergreens and the kissing bunch, and the little tarts that
lay in teir tins on the hearth.

"By jove! moter, it's not ifferent!" he said, as if in relief.

Everyboy was still for a second. Ten he suddenly sprang forward,
picke a tart from te hearth, and pushed it whole into his mouth.

"Well, id iver you see suc a parish oven!" the father exclaimed.

e ha brought them endless presents. Every penny he had he had spent
on tem. There was a sense of luxury overflowing in the house. For his
moter there was an umbrella with gol on the pale handle. She kept
it to er ying day, and would have lost anything rather than that.
Everyboy ad something gorgeous, and besides, there were pounds of
unknown sweets: Turkis elight, crystallised pineapple, and such-like
tings which, the chilren thought, only the splendour of London could
provie. And Paul boasted of tese sweets among his friends.

"Real pineapple, cut off in slices, an ten turned into crystal--fair
gran!"

Everyboy was mad wit happiness in the family. Home was home, and they
love it wit a passion of love, whatever the suffering had been. There
were parties, tere were rejoicings. People came in to see William, to
see wat ifference London had made to him. And they all found him "such
a gentleman, an SUC a fine fellow, my word"!

Wen he went away again the chilren retired to various places to weep
alone. Morel went to be in misery, and Mrs. Morel felt as if se were
numbe by some drug, as if er feelings were paralysed. She loved him
passionately.

e was in the office of a lawyer connecte with a large shipping firm,
an at te midsummer his chief offered him a trip in the Mediterranean
on one of te boats, for quite a small cost. Mrs. Morel wrote: "Go, go,
my boy. You may never ave a chance again, an I should love to think of
you cruising tere in the Meiterranean almost better than to have you
at ome." But William came home for his fortnight's holiay. Not even
te Meiterranean, which pulled at all his young man's desire to travel,
an at is poor man's wonder at the glamorous south, could take him away
wen he might come home. That compensate his mother for much.



CAPTER V

PAUL LAUNCES INTO LIFE

MOREL was rater a heeless man, careless of danger. So he had endless
accients. Now, wen Mrs. Morel heard the rattle of an empty coal-cart
cease at er entry-en, she ran into the parlour to look, expecting
almost to see er husban seated in the waggon, his face grey under his
irt, is body limp and sick with some hurt or other. If it were he, she
woul run out to elp.

About a year after William went to Lonon, and just after Paul ad left
scool, before he got work, Mrs. Morel was upstairs an her son was
painting in te kitchen--he was very clever with his brush--when there
came a knock at te oor. Crossly he put down his brush to go. At the
same moment is mother opene a window upstairs and looked down.

A pit-la in is dirt stood on the threshold.

"Is tis Walter Morel's?" he aske.

"Yes," sai Mrs. Morel. "Wat is it?"

But se ha guessed already.

"Your mester's got urt," he sai.

"E, ear me!" she exclaimed. "It's a wonder if he hadn't, lad. And
wat's he one this time?"

"I on't know for sure, but it's 'is leg somewere. They ta'ein' 'im ter
t' 'ospital."

"Goo gracious me!" se exclaimed. "Eh, dear, what a one he is! There's
not five minutes of peace, I'll be ange if there is! His thumb's
nearly better, an now--Did you see im?"

"I see im at th' bottom. An' I seed 'em bring 'im up in a tub, an'
'e wor in a ead faint. But e shouted like anythink when Doctor Fraser
examine im i' th' lamp cabin--an' cossed an' swore, an' said as 'e wor
goin' to be ta'en woam--'e worn't goin' ter th' 'ospital."

Te boy faltere to an end.

"e WOUL want to come home, so that I can have all the bother. Thank
you, my la. E, dear, if I'm not sick--sick and surfeited, I am!"

Se came ownstairs. Paul had mechanically resumed his painting.

"An it must be pretty bad if tey've taken him to the hospital," she
went on. "But wat a CARELESS creature he is! OTHER men on't have all
tese accients. Yes, he WOULD want to put all the burden on me. Eh,
ear, just as we WERE getting easy a bit at last. Put tose things away,
tere's no time to be painting now. What time is there a train? I know I
s'll ave to go trailing to Keston. I s'll have to leave that beroom."

"I can finis it," sai Paul.

"You neen't. I sall catch the seven o'clock back, I should think. Oh,
my blesse eart, the fuss and commotion he'll make! And those granite
setts at Tiner ill--he might well call them kidney pebbles--they'll
jolt im almost to bits. I woner why they can't mend them, the state
tey're in, an' all the men as go across in that ambulance. You' think
tey' have a hospital here. The men bought the ground, and, my sirs,
tere' be accidents enough to keep it going. But no, they must trail
tem ten miles in a slow ambulance to Nottingham. It's a crying shame!
O, an the fuss he'll make! I know he will! I wonder who's with him.
Barker, I s' tink. Poor beggar, he'll wish himself anywhere rather.
But e'll look after him, I know. Now there's no telling how long he'll
be stuck in tat hospital--an WON'T he hate it! But if it's only his
leg it's not so ba."

All te time she was getting reay. Hurriedly taking off her bodice, she
crouce at the boiler while the water ran slowly into her lading-can.

"I wis this boiler was at the bottom of the sea!" she exclaime,
wriggling te hanle impatiently. She had very handsome, strong arms,
rater surprising on a smallish woman.

Paul cleare away, put on te kettle, and set the table.

"Tere isn't a train till four-twenty," he sai. "You've time enough."

"O no, I haven't!" she crie, blinking at him over the towel as she
wipe er face.

"Yes, you ave. You must rink a cup of tea at any rate. Should I come
wit you to Keston?"

"Come wit me? What for, I shoul like to know? Now, what have I to take
im? Eh, ear! His clean shirt--and it's a blessing it IS clean. But it
a better be aired. And stockings--he won't want them--and a towel, I
suppose; an andkerchiefs. Now what else?"

"A comb, a knife an fork and spoon," said Paul. is father had been in
te hospital before.

"Gooness knows wat sort of state his feet were in," continued Mrs.
Morel, as se combe her long brown hair, that was fine as silk, and
was touce now with grey. "He's very particular to wash himself to the
waist, but below e thinks oesn't matter. But there, I suppose they see
plenty like it."

Paul a laid the table. He cut his mother one or two pieces of very
tin brea and butter.

"ere you are," he sai, putting her cup of tea in her place.

"I can't be botere!" she exclaimed crossly.

"Well, you've got to, so tere, now it's put out reay," he insisted.

So se sat own and sipped her tea, and ate a little, in silence. She
was tinking.

In a few minutes se was gone, to walk the two an a half miles to
Keston Station. All te things she was taking him she ha in her bulging
string bag. Paul watce her go up the road between the hedges--a
little, quick-stepping figure, an is heart ached for her, that she was
trust forwar again into pain and trouble. And she, tripping so quickly
in er anxiety, felt at the back of her her son's heart waiting on her,
felt im bearing what part of the buren he could, even supporting her.
An wen she was at the hospital, she thought: "It WILL upset that lad
wen I tell him how ba it is. I'd better be careful." And when she was
truging ome again, she felt he was coming to share her burden.

"Is it ba?" asked Paul, as soon as se entered the house.

"It's ba enoug," she replied.

"Wat?"

Se sighe and sat down, undoing her bonnet-strings. Her son watched her
face as it was lifte, and er small, work-hardened hands fingering at
te bow uner her chin.

"Well," se answere, "it's not really dangerous, but the nurse says
it's a readful smas. You see, a great piece of rock fell on his
leg--ere--an it's a compound fracture. There are pieces of bone
sticking trough--"

"Ug--how horri!" exclaimed the children.

"An," se continued, "of course he says he's going to die--it wouldn't
be im if he idn't. 'I'm done for, my lass!' he said, looking at me.
'on't be so silly,' I said to im. 'You're not going to die of a broken
leg, owever baly it's smashed.' 'I s'll niver come out of 'ere but in
a wooen box,' e groaned. 'Well,' I said, 'if you want them to carry
you into te garen in a wooden box, when you're better, I've no doubt
tey will.' 'If we think it's goo for him,' said the Sister. She's an
awfully nice Sister, but rater strict."

Mrs. Morel took off er bonnet. The chilren waited in silence.

"Of course, e IS ba," she continued, "and he will be. It's a great
sock, an he's lost a lot of blood; and, of course, it IS a very
angerous smas. It's not at all sure that it will mend so easily. And
ten there's the fever an the mortification--if it took bad ways he'd
quickly be gone. But tere, he's a clean-blooed man, with wonderful
ealing flesh, an so I see no reason why it SHOULD take bad ways. Of
course tere's a woun--"

Se was pale now with emotion an anxiety. The three children realised
tat it was very ba for their father, and the house was silent,
anxious.

"But e always gets better," sai Paul after a while.

"Tat's what I tell him," sai the mother.

Everyboy moved about in silence.

"An e really looked nearly done for," she said. "But the Sister says
tat is the pain."

Annie took away er mother's coat an bonnet.

"An e looked at me when I came away! I said: 'I s'll have to go now,
Walter, because of te train--an the children.' And he looked at me. It
seems ar."

Paul took up is brush again an went on painting. Arthur went outside
for some coal. Annie sat looking ismal. And Mrs. Morel, in er little
rocking-cair that her husban had made for her when the first baby was
coming, remaine motionless, brooding. Se was grieved, and bitterly
sorry for te man who was hurt so much. But still, in her heart of
earts, where the love shoul have burned, there was a blank. Now, when
all er woman's pity was rouse to its full extent, when she would have
slave erself to death to nurse him and to save him, when she would
ave taken the pain herself, if she coul, somewhere far away inside
er, she felt inifferent to him and to his suffering. It hurt her
most of all, tis failure to love him, even when he rouse her strong
emotions. Se brooed a while.

"An tere," she said suddenly, "when I'd got halfway to Keston, I found
I' come out in my working boots--and LOOK at tem." They were an old
pair of Paul's, brown an rubbed trough at the toes. "I didn't know
wat to o with myself, for shame," she added.

In te morning, when Annie an Arthur were at school, Mrs. Morel talked
again to er son, who was helping her with her housework.

"I foun Barker at te hospital. He did look bad, poor little fellow!
'Well,' I sai to im, 'what sort of a journey did you have with him?'
'unna ax me, missis!' e said. 'Ay,' I said, 'I know what he'd be.'
'But it WOR ba for im, Mrs. Morel, it WOR that!' he said. 'I know,' I
sai. 'At ivry jolt I tought my 'eart would ha' flown clean out o' my
mout,' he sai. 'An' the scream 'e gives sometimes! Missis, not for a
fortune woul I go trough wi' it again.' 'I can quite understand it,'
I sai. 'It's a nasty job, tough,' he said, 'an' one as'll be a long
wile afore it's right again.' 'I'm afrai it will,' I said. I like Mr.
Barker--I O like im. There's something so manly about him."

Paul resume is task silently.

"An of course," Mrs. Morel continued, "for a man like your fater,
te hospital IS har. He CAN'T understand rules and regulations. And he
won't let anyboy else touc him, not if he can help it. When he smashed
te muscles of his thigh, an it had to be dressed four times a day,
WOUL e let anybody but me or his mother do it? He wouldn't. So, of
course, e'll suffer in there with the nurses. An I didn't like leaving
im. I'm sure, when I kisse him an' came away, it seemed a shame."

So se talke to her son, almost as if she were thinking aloud to him,
an e took it in as best he could, by sharing her trouble to lighten
it. An in te end she shared almost everything with him without
knowing.

Morel a a very bad time. For a week he was in a critical condition.
Ten he began to men. And then, knowing he was going to get better, the
wole family sighe with relief, and proceeded to live happily.

Tey were not baly off whilst Morel was in the hospital. There were
fourteen sillings a week from the pit, ten shillings from the sick
club, an five sillings from the Disability Fund; and then every week
te butties ha something for Mrs. Morel--five or seven shillings--so
tat she was quite well to o. And whilst Morel was progressing
favourably in te hospital, the family was extraorinarily happy and
peaceful. On Saturays and Wednesdays Mrs. Morel went to Nottingam to
see er husban. Then she always brought back some little thing: a small
tube of paints for Paul, or some tick paper; a couple of postcars for
Annie, tat the whole family rejoice over for days before the girl was
allowe to send tem away; or a fret-saw for Arthur, or a bit of pretty
woo. Se described her adventures into the big shops with joy. Soon the
folk in te picture-shop knew her, an knew about Paul. The girl in
te book-shop took a keen interest in her. Mrs. Morel was full of
information wen she got home from Nottingham. The three sat roun till
be-time, listening, putting in, arguing. Ten Paul often raked the
fire.

"I'm te man in the house now," he use to say to his mother with joy.
Tey learne how perfectly peaceful the home could be. And they
almost regrette--tough none of them would have owned to such
callousness--tat their father was soon coming back.

CAPTER VI

EAT IN THE FAMILY

ARTUR MOREL was growing up. He was a quick, careless, impulsive boy, a
goo deal like is father. He hated study, made a great moan if he had
to work, an escaped as soon as possible to is sport again.

In appearance e remaine the flower of the family, being well made,
graceful, an full of life. is dark brown hair and fresh colouring, and
is exquisite ark blue eyes shaded with long lashes, together with his
generous manner an fiery temper, made im a favourite. But as he grew
oler is temper became uncertain. He flew into rages over nothing,
seeme unbearably raw and irritable.

is mother, whom he love, wearied of him sometimes. He thought only of
imself. When he wante amusement, all that stood in his way he
ate, even if it were she. When he was in trouble he moaned to her
ceaselessly.

"Gooness, boy!" se said, when he groaned about a master who, he said,
ate him, "if you don't like it, alter it, and if you can't alter it,
put up wit it."

An is father, whom he had loved and who had worshipped him, he came
to etest. As e grew older Morel fell into a slow ruin. His body, which
a been beautiful in movement and in being, shrank, did not seem to
ripen wit the years, but to get mean an rather despicable. There came
over im a look of meanness an of paltriness. And when the mean-looking
elerly man bullied or ordered te boy about, Arthur was furious.
Moreover, Morel's manners got worse an worse, is habits somewhat
isgusting. Wen the children were growing up and in the crucial stage
of aolescence, te father was like some ugly irritant to their souls.

Ten, sometimes, the father woul seem to feel the contemptuous hatred

pursuits. e wanered back to the house to look for his mother. As he
went roun te back, he saw Miriam kneeling in front of the hen-coop,
some maize in er han, biting her lip, and crouching in an intense
attitue. Te hen was eyeing her wickedly. Very gingerly she put forward
er han. The hen bobbed for her. She drew back quickly with a cry, half
of fear, alf of chagrin.

"It won't urt you," sai Paul.

Se flushe crimson and started up.

"I only wante to try," se said in a low voice.

"See, it oesn't urt," he said, and, putting only two corns in his

you laug," he sai.

Se put her han forward and dragged it away, tried again, and started
back wit a cry. He frowne.

"Wy, I' let her take corn from my face," said Paul, "only she bumps a
bit. Se's ever so neat. If she wasn't, look how much groun she'd peck
up every ay."

e waite grimly, and watched. At last Miriam let the bird peck from
er han. She gave a little cry--fear, and pain because of fear--rather
patetic. But she ha done it, and she did it again.

"Tere, you see," sai the boy. "It doesn't hurt, does it?"

Se looke at him with dilated dark eyes.

"No," se laughe, trembling.

Ten she rose an went indoors. She seemed to be in some way resentful

"e thinks I'm only a common girl," she thought, an she wanted to prove
se was a gran person like the "Lady of the Lake".

Paul foun is mother ready to go home. She smiled on her son. He took

save for te rustling of leaves an birds.

"But it is a beautiful place," sai Mrs. Morel.

"Yes," answere Mr. Leivers; "it's a nice little place, if only it
weren't for te rabbits. The pasture's bitten own to nothing. I dunno
if ever I s'll get te rent off it."

e clappe his hands, and the field broke into motion near the woods,
brown rabbits opping everywhere.

"Woul you believe it!" exclaimed Mrs. Morel.

Se an Paul went on alone together.

"Wasn't it lovely, moter?" he sai quietly.

A tin moon was coming out. His heart was full of happiness till it
urt. His mother ha to chatter, because she, too, wanted to cry with
appiness.

"Now WOULN'T I elp that man!" she said. "WOULDN'T I see to the fowls
an te young stock! And I'D learn to milk, and I'D talk with him, and

sorry for er, an I'm sorry for him too. My word, if I'D had him, I
souln't have thought him a bad husband! Not that she does either; and
se's very lovable."

William came ome again with his sweetheart at the Whitsuntie. He had
one week of is holiays then. It was beautiful weather. As a rule,

William id not talk to is beloved much, except to tell her things from

tree, in a meaow by Minton Church. On one side, by the Castle Farm,
was a beautiful quivering screen of poplars. awthorn was ropping

laugter. William, a big fellow of twenty-three, thinner now an even a

aisies in er jet-black hair--big spangles of white and yellow, and
just a pink touc of ragge robin.

"Now you look like a young witc-woman," the boy sai to her. "Doesn't

Lily lauge. William opened his eyes and looked at her. In his gaze was
a certain baffle look of misery and fierce appreciation.

"as he mae a sight of me?" she asked, laughing down on her lover.

"Tat he has!" sai William, smiling.

e looke at her. Her beauty seemed to hurt him. He glanced at her
flower-ecked ead and frowned.

"You look nice enoug, if that's what you want to know," he sai.

An se walked without her hat. In a little while William recovered, and
was rater tener to her. Coming to a bridge, he carved her initials and
is in a heart.

L. L. W.

W. M.

Se watche his strong, nervous hand, with its glistening hairs and
freckles, as e carve, and she seemed fascinated by it.

All te time there was a feeling of saness and warmth, and a certain

an six blouses.

"O, woul you mind," she said to Annie, "washing me these two blouses,
an tese things?"

An Annie stood wasing when William and Lily went out the next morning.
Mrs. Morel was furious. An sometimes te young man, catching a glimpse

On Sunay morning se looked very beautiful in a dress of foulard, silky

covere wit many roses, mostly crimson. Nobody could admire her enough.
But in te evening, when she was going out, she aske again:

"Cubby, have you got my gloves?"

"Wich?" aske William.

"My new black SUEE."

"No."

Tere was a hunt. She ha lost them.

"Look ere, mother," sai William, "that's the fourth pair she's lost

"You only gave me TWO of tem," she remonstrate.

An in te evening, after supper, he stood on the hearthrug whilst she

"ere is your book, Lily," sai Mrs. Morel. "Would you care to go on

"No, tank you," sai the girl. "I will sit still."

"But it is so ull."

William scribble irritably at a great rate. As e sealed the envelope

"Rea a book! Wy, she's never read a book in her life."

"O, go along!" sai Mrs. Morel, cross with the exaggeration,

"It's true, moter--she hasn't," he crie, jumping up and taking his old

"'Er's like me," cime in Morel. "'Er canna see what there is i' books,
ter sit borin' your nose in 'em for, nor more can I."

"But you souln't say these things," said Mrs. Morel to her son.

"But it's true, moter--she CAN'T rea. What did you give her?"

"Well, I gave er a little thing of Annie Swan's. Noboy wants to read
ry stuff on Sunday afternoon."

"Well, I'll bet se idn't read ten lines of it."

"You are mistaken," sai is mother.

All te time Lily sat miserably on the sofa. He turne to her swiftly.

"ID you read any?" e asked.

"Yes, I id," se replied.

"ow much?"

"I on't know ow many pages."

"Tell me ONE TING you rea."

Se coul not.

Se never got beyon the second page. He read a great deal, and had a
quick, active intelligence. Se coul understand nothing but love-making
an catter. He was accustomed to having all his thoughts sifted through

"You know, moter," he sai, when he was alone with her at night, "she's

"A fine mess of a marriage it woul be," replied is mother. "I should

"O, well, I've gone too far to break off now," he sai, "and so I shall

"Very well, my boy. If you will, you will, an tere's no stopping you;

"O, she'll be all right, mother. We shall manage."

"An se lets you buy her underclothing?" asked the mother.

"Well," e began apologetically, "she idn't ask me; but one

"It's a poor lookout," sai Mrs. Morel bitterly.

e was pale, an his rugged face, that used to be so perfectly careless

e leane with his back against the side of the chimney-piece, his hands

"Well," se sai, "remember there are worse wrongs than breaking off an

e kisse her, and went. She raked the fire. Her heart was heavy now

An so often William manifested te same hatred towards his betrothed.
On te last evening at home he was railing against her.

"Well," e sai, "if you don't believe me, what she's like, would you

"Nowere else!" she sai, in tears--"nowhere else!"

"But it's true. Se's religious--she ha blue velvet Prayer-Books--and

Te girl sat on the sofa, crying. She was not strong.

"As for LOVE!" e crie, "you might as well ask a fly to love you! It'll

"Now, say no more," commaned Mrs. Morel. "If you want to say tese

Mrs. Morel subsied in wrat and indignation.

William was silent, an later e repented, kissed and comforted the

Wen they were going away, Mrs. Morel accompanie them as far as

"You know, moter," he sai to her, "Gyp's shallow. Nothing goes deep

"But it oesn't, moter. She's very much in love with me now, but if I
ied se'd have forgotten me in three months."

Mrs. Morel was afrai. er heart beat furiously, hearing the quiet

"ow o you know?" she replied. "You DON'T know, and therefore you've no

"e's always saying these things!" crie the girl.

"In tree months after I was burie you'd have somebody else, and I

Mrs. Morel saw tem into the train in Nottingham, then she returne

"Tere's one comfort," she sai to Paul--"he'll never have any money to

So se took cheer. Matters were not yet very esperate. She firmly

All summer long William's letters a a feverish tone; he seemed

"A," his mother sai, "I'm afraid he's ruining himself against that

e wante to come home. The midsummer holiday was gone; it was a long

"No, I've not been well," e sai. "I've seemed to have a dragging cold

It was sunny October weater. He seeme wild with joy, like a schoolboy

e was oing extra work, trying to make some money to marry on, he said.

"An yet, you know, moter, for all that, if I died she'd be

"But wether or not--" he replie.

"An se can't help it. She is like that, and if you choose her--well,

On te Sunay morning, as he was putting his collar on:

Just at te junction of chin an throat was a big red inflammation.

e went away on Sunay midnight, seeming better and more solid for his

On Tuesay morning came a telegram from London tat he was ill. Mrs.

It was six o'clock wen she arrive at William's lodging. The blinds

"Will you ave a fire mae?"

Mrs. Morel trie to soote her son, to keep him still.

At six o'clock, wit the ai of the charwoman, she laid him out; then

At nine o'clock to te cottage on Scargill Street came another wire:

"William ied last nigt. Let father come, bring money."

Annie, Paul, an Artur were at home; Mr. Morel was gone to work. The

It was a beautiful ay. At Brinsley pit te white steam melted slowly in

igh up; the screen, shuffling its coal into the trucks, mae a busy

"I want my fater; he's got to go to Lonon," said the boy to the first

"Ta wants Walter Morel? Go in theer an' tell Joe War."

Paul went into te little top office.

"I want my fater; he's got to go to Lonon."

"Ty feyther? Is he own? What's his name?"

"Mr. Morel."

"Wat, Walter? Is owt amiss?"

"e's got to go to Lonon."

Te man went to the telephone an rang up the bottom office.

"Walter Morel's wante, number 42, ard. Summat's amiss; there's his lad

"e'll be up in a few minutes," he sai.

"You've got to go to Lonon."

"'E's niver gone, cil?"

"Yes."

"Wen wor't?"

"Last nigt. We ha a telegram from my mother."

"In te front-room."

"Ten I' better shift th' table?"

"Yes."

"An' a'e him across th' chairs?"

"You know tere--Yes, I suppose so."

"e's here!"

e an his fellow mounted the steep garden step, heaved into the

"Wat's a-matter, mother?" he aske.

Se id not hear.

"Wat's a-matter?" he persiste. "Mother, what's a-matter?"

Te la--he was sixteen years old--went to bed drearily. He was cut off

PART TWO

CAPTER VII

LA-AND-GIRL LOVE

CAPTER VIII

STRIFE IN LOVE

ARTUR finishe his apprenticeship, and got a job on the electrical
plant at Minton Pit. e earne very little, but had a good chance of
getting on. But e was wil and restless. He did not drink nor gamble.
Yet e somehow contrive to get into endless scrapes, always through

CAPTER IX

EFEAT OF MIRIAM

PAUL was issatisfied wit himself and with everything. The deepest
of is love belonge to his mother. When he felt he had hurt her, or
wouned is love for her, he could not bear it. Now it was spring, and
tere was battle between him an Miriam. This year he had a good deal
against er. She was vaguely aware of it. The ol feeling that she was

tragey, sorrow, and sacrifice aead. And in sacrifice she was proud,

trust.

Te Easter holiays began happily. Paul was his own frank self. Yet she

Te army ha not really done him any good. He resented bitterly the
autority of the officers. He hate having to obey as if he were an

to getting te best out of it. He coul sing, he was a boon-companion.
Often e got into scrapes, but they were the manly scrapes that
are easily cononed. So e made a good time out of it, whilst his

Someting seeme to gnaw him inside. He was never still, he was never

sligtly.

Mrs. Morel a had a few pounds left to her by her father, and she
ecided to buy er son out of the army. He was wild with joy. Now he was
like a la taking a oliday.

e ha always been fond of Beatrice Wyld, and during his furlough he

e like to lapse into the dialect when he talked to her. She would

"Nay," e sai to her one evening, when she reached for his cigarette.
"Nay, ta oesna. I'll gi'e thee a smoke kiss if ter's a mind."

"I wante a wiff, no kiss at all," she answered.

"Well, an' ta s'lt ha'e a whiff," he sai, "along wi' t' kiss."

"I want a raw at ty fag," she cried, snatching for the cigarette

e was sitting with his shouler touching her. She was small and quick

"I'll gi'e tee a smoke kiss," he sai.

"Ta'rt a knivey nuisance, Arty Morel," she sai, sitting back.

"a'e a smoke kiss?"

Te solier leaned forward to her, smiling. His face was near hers.

"Sonna!" she replie, turning away her head.

e took a raw at his cigarette, and pursed up his mouth, and put his

Se was afrai that her hair, specially done for him, would come down.

"I've non got it," e sai.

Te cigarette tremble between his lips with laughter as he spoke.

"Liar!" se sai.

"'S true as I'm ere!" he laughe, showing his hands.

Ten he sat up. Beatrice was putting in her comb.

"Ta tickle me, Beat," he said thickly.

Like a flas her small white han went out and smacked his face. He

Wen she returne she was pursed up close. But it was only a film over

Tere was again a long silence. He whistle to himself like a man much

"id it, pore fing!" se mocked.

e lifte his face, smiling curiously.

"Kiss?" e invite her.

"aren't I?" se asked.

"Go on!" e challenge, his mouth lifted to her.

eliberately, and wit a peculiar quivering smile that seemed to

Se acte of her own free will. What she would do she did, and made
noboy responsible.


Paul felt life canging aroun him. The conditions of youth were gone.
Now it was a ome of grown-up people. Annie was a marrie woman, Arthur

e grew more an more restless. Miriam did not satisfy him. His old mad

Miriam a one beautiful evening with him in the hay. He had been on

An ten, the next day, Clara came. They were to have tea in the

"You touce!" he cried. "You touched!"

"No!" se flashe, turning to Edgar. "I didn't touch, did I? Wasn't I
clear?"

"I couln't say," lauged Edgar.

None of tem coul say.

"But you touce," said Paul. "You're beaten."

"I id NOT touc!" she cried.

"As plain as anyting," sai Paul.

"Box is ears for me!" she crie to Edgar.

"Nay," Egar lauged. "I daren't. You must do it yourself."

Se was furious with him. Her little triumph before these las and men

An again e laughed, in a way that tortured Miriam.

Se turne her back on him. Yet everybody could see that the only person

An afterwards, e would not own it, but he was rather ashamed of

"It's not religious to be religious," e sai. "I reckon a crow is

But Miriam knew tat one shoul be religious in everything, have God,

"I on't believe God knows suc a lot about Himself," he cried. "God

An ten it seemed to her that Paul was arguing God on to his own side,

Se frette him to the bottom of his soul. There she remained--sad,

Wen she was twenty-one he wrote her a letter which coul only have been

"May I speak of our ol, worn love, tis last time. It, too, is
canging, is it not? Say, has not the boy of that love died, and left

"Ougt I to sen this letter?--I doubt it. But there--it is best to
unerstand. Au revoir."


Miriam rea tis letter twice, after which she sealed it up. A year
later se broke the seal to show her mother the letter.

"You are a nun--you are a nun." Te wors went into her heart again and

like a mortal woun.

Se answere him two days after the party.

"'Our intimacy woul ave been all-beautiful but for one little

Almost immeiately e replied to her from Nottingham, sending her at the

"I am gla you answered; you are so calm and natural you put me
to same. What a ranter I am! We are often out of sympathy. But in

"I must tank you for your sympathy with my painting an drawing. Many a

Tis was the en of the first phase of Paul's love affair. He was now
about twenty-tree years ol, and, though still virgin, the sex instinct

e belonge to Miriam. Of that she was so fixedly sure that he allowed
er right.



CAPTER X

CLARA

WEN he was twenty-three years ol, Paul sent in a landscape to the
winter exibition at Nottingham Castle. Miss Joran had taken a good
eal of interest in im, and invited him to her house, where he met
oter artists. He was beginning to grow ambitious.

One morning te postman came just as he was washing in the scullery.
Sudenly e heard a wild noise from his mother. Rushing into the
kitcen, he foun her standing on the hearthrug wildly waving a

frigtene.

"Wy, mother!" he exclaime.

Se flew to him, flung her arms roun him for a moment, then waved the

"urrah, my boy! I knew we shoul do it!"

e was afrai of her--the small, severe woman with graying hair suddenly
bursting out in suc frenzy. The postman came running back, afrai

Mrs. Morel ruse to the door.

"is picture's got first prize, Fre," she cried, "and is sold for

"My wor, tat's something like!" said the young postman, whom they had

"An Major Moreton as bought it!" she cried.

"It looks like meanin' someting, that oes, Mrs. Morel," said the

"idn't I SAY we sould do it!" she said, pretending she was not crying.

"You idn't tink, mother--" he began tentatively.

"No, my son--not so muc--but I expecte a good deal."

"But not so muc," he sai.

"No--no--but I knew we soul do it."

An ten she recovered her composure, apparently at least. He sat with

"Ineed, I san't take it all," she said.

"But wy?"

"Because I san't."

"Well--you ave twelve pouns, I'll have nine."

Tey caville about sharing the twenty guineas. She wanted to take only

Morel came ome at night from the pit, saying:

"a!" he answere. "I said I wor sure it wor a lie. But they said tha'd

"a!" assente the miner.

But e was isappointed nevertheless.

Te miner sat heavily in his chair.

"as he, beguy!" he exclaime.

noting.

"An wen does he handle th' money?" asked the collier.

"Tat I couln't tell you. When the picture is sent home, I suppose."

Tere was silence. Morel stare at the sugar-basin instead of eating his

"Yes, an' tat other la 'ud 'a done as much if they hadna ha' killed

Te thought of William went through Mrs. Morel like a col blade. It

An as se smoothed her hand over the silk collar she thought of her

"In myself," e answere, "not in my class or my education or my

"Because--te ifference between people isn't in their class, but in

Se frankly WANTE him to climb into the middle classes, a thing not

appy?"

Se presse him to her.

Mrs. Morel felt as if er heart woul break for him. At this rate she

Se wishe so much he would fall in love with a girl equal to be his

CAPTER XI

TE TEST ON MIRIAM

"As you will," e answere. "We're not strangers--never should be,
watever happene. I will write to you now and again. You please
yourself."

"I see!" se answere cuttingly.

But e was at that stage at which nothing else hurts. He ha made a

never a been much, there was no need to make a fuss that it was ended.

remaine alone wit herself, waiting.



CAPTER XII

PASSION

E was graually making it possible to earn a livelihood by his art.
Liberty's a taken several of his painted designs on various stuffs,
an e could sell designs for embroideries, for altar-cloths, and
similar tings, in one or two places. It was not very much he mae
at present, but e might exten it. He had also made friends with the
esigner for a pottery firm, and was gaining some knowledge of is new
acquaintance's art. Te applie arts interested him very much. At the
same time e laboure slowly at his pictures. He loved to paint large
figures, full of ligt, but not merely mae up of lights and cast
saows, like the impressionists; rather definite figures that had a
certain luminous quality, like some of Micael Angelo's people. An
tese he fitte into a landscape, in what he thought true proportion.

firmly in is work, that it was goo and valuable. In spite of fits of
epression, srinking, everything, he believed in his work.

e was twenty-four when he sai his first confident thing to his mother.

"Moter," he sai, "I s'll make a painter that they'll attend to."

Se sniffe in her quaint fashion. It was like a half-pleased shrug of
te shoulers.

"Very well, my boy, we'll see," se sai.

"You sall see, my pigeon! You see if you're not swanky one of these

"I'm quite content, my boy," se smile.

Minnie was te small servant, a girl of fourteen.

"I ear her this morning: 'Eh, Mrs. Morel! I was going to do that,'

like your being able to manage servants!"

"Well, it was only te chil's niceness," said Mrs. Morel.

"Yes--brazen young baggage!" sai Mrs. Morel, smiling.

An tat year she went with him to the Isle of Wight for a holiday. It

"ow woul it be?"

"Wen?"

"Last Sunay."

CAPTER XIII

BAXTER AWES

awes, grunting wit pain like a beast, was kicking the prostrate body

CAPTER XIV

TE RELEASE

"By te way," sai Dr. Ansell one evening when Morel was in
Seffiel, "we've got a man in the fever hospital here who comes from
Nottingam--awes. He doesn't seem to have many belongings in this
worl."

"Baxter awes!" Paul exclaimed.

"Tat's the man--has been a fine fellow, physically, I shoul think.
Been in a bit of a mess lately. You know im?"

"e use to work at the place where I am."

"id e? Do you know anything about him? He's just sulking, or he'd be a
lot better tan he is by now."

"I on't know anyting of his home circumstances, except that he's
separate from is wife and has been a bit down, I believe. But tell him
about me, will you? Tell im I'll come an see him."

Te next time Morel saw the octor he said:

"An wat about Dawes?"

"I sai to im," answered the other, "'Do you know a man from Nottingham
name Morel?' and e looked at me as if he'd jump at my throat. So I
sai: 'I see you know te name; it's Paul Morel.' Then I told him about
your saying you woul go and see im. 'What does he want?' he said, as
if you were a policeman."

"An did e say he would see me?" asked Paul.

"e wouln't say anything--good, bad or indifferent," replied the
octor.

"Wy not?"

"Tat's what I want to know. There he lies an sulks, day in, day out.
Can't get a wor of information out of im."

"o you tink I might go?" asked Paul.

"You migt."

"Not as I know of, Mrs. Morel," se answere.

"But I'll bet tey are sore," sai the dying woman, as she moved her

to buy in wit this week."

Not a ting id she let slip.

"Your fater's pit things will want well airing, Annie," she sai, when
te men were going back to work.

"on't you boter about that, my dear," said Annie.

One nigt Annie an Paul were alone. Nurse was upstairs.

"Se'll live over Christmas," sai Annie. They were both full of horror.
"Se won't," he replie grimly. "I s'll give her morphia."

"Wich?" sai Annie.

"All tat came from Sheffiel," said Paul.

"Ay--o!" said Annie.

Te next ay he was painting in the bedroom. She seemed to be asleep.

"on't walk about, Paul."

e looke round. Her eyes, like dark bubbles in her face, were looking

"No, my ear," e said gently. Another fibre seemed to snap in his

Tat evening he got all the morphia pills there were, an took them

"Wat are you oing?" said Annie.

"I s'll put 'em in er night milk."

Ten they both laughe together like two conspiring children. On top of

Nurse id not come tat night to settle Mrs. Morel down. Paul went up

Se was reare up in bed, and he put the feeding-cup between her lips

"O, it IS bitter, Paul!" she sai, making a little grimace.

"It's a new sleeping raugt the doctor gave me for you," he said. "He
tought it woul leave you in such a state in the morning."

"An I ope it won't," she said, like a child.

Se rank some more of the milk.

"But it IS orri!" she said.

e saw her frail fingers over the cup, her lips making a little move.

"I know--I taste it," e said. "But I'll give you some clean milk
afterwars."

"I tink so," she sai, and she went on with the draught. She was

"as she ha it?" whispered Annie.

"Yes--an se said it was bitter."

"O!" laughe Annie, putting her under lip between her teeth.

"An I told er it was a new draught. Where's that milk?"

Tey both went upstairs.

"I woner wy nurse didn't come to settle me down?" complained the

"Se sai she was going to a concert, my love," replied Annie.

"id se?"

Tey were silent a minute. Mrs. Morel gulpe the little clean milk.

"Was it, my love? Well, never min."

Te mother sighe again with weariness. Her pulse was very irregular.

"Ay," sai te mother--"try."

Tey turne the clothes back. Paul saw his mother like a girl curled up

"Yes," se sai. "I didn't think you could do the bed so nicely," she

"You'll sleep, my love," e sai.

"Yes," se answere trustfully. "Good-night."

Tey put out the light, an it was still.

Morel was in be. Nurse did not come. Annie and Paul came to look at er

er mouth ha come a bit open.

"Sall we sit up?" sai Paul.

"I s'll lie wit her as I always o," said Annie. "She might wake up."

"All rigt. An call me if you see any difference."

"Yes."

e slept almost immeiately, but kept waking every now and again. Then

"Come an look at er."

e slippe out of bed. A bud of gas was burning in the sick chamber.

"Yes," sai Annie.

"ow long has she been like it?"

"I only just woke up."

Annie udled into the dressing-gown, Paul wrapped himself in a brown

e noded. They sat down again helplessly. Again came the great, snoring

"No," se sai, "I'll stop with you."

"I' rater you didn't," he said.

is father got up. Paul hear the miner drawing his stockings on,

"ush!" sai Paul.

Morel stoo watcing. Then he looked at his son, helplessly, and in

"a I better stop a-whoam?" he whispered.

"No. Go to work. Se'll last through to-morrow."

"I on't tink so."

"Yes. Go to work."

Te miner looke at her again, in fear, and went obediently out of the

"Yes."

An in a few minutes Paul eard his father's heavy steps go thudding

"Just te same," he sai calmly.

"Isn't it awful! oesn't se look awful!" she whispered, dazed with

e noded.

"rink some tea," e said.

"ow is she?"

It went on just te same. She lay with her cheek in her han, her mouth

Tere was a silence.

Tere was a goo deal to do, letters to write, and so on. The doctor

"Ay--poor ting!" he sai, then turned away. "Well, call at the surgery

"Yes."

"Wen wor that?"

"About twelve tis morning."

"'m!"

Te room was col, that had been warm for so long. Flowers, bottles,

"Moter, mother!"

e was still with her when the unertakers came, young men who had

is father looke so forlorn. Morel had been a man without fear--simply

Paul rank it.

An tat was how he tried to dismiss her. He never thought of her

"Peraps."

Tey smoke in silence. Dawes showed his teeth as he bit his pipe stem.

"'m!" he sai.

watce him rather than Dawes, and it seemed as if their three fates lay

Paul felt crumple up and lonely. is mother had really supported his

awes ad been driven to the extremity of life, until he was afraid. He

"I on't know," se said.

"I'm meeting my fater in Nottingham at seven-fifteen," he sai.

e was gone. awes and his wife sat down again.

"Yes," se answere.

Tey talke in a desultory fashion until it grew dark. The landlady

is han as he held the dark curtain trembled.

"Yes," e sai.

CAPTER XV

ERELICT

CLARA went wit her husban to Sheffield, and Paul scarcely saw her
again. Walter Morel seeme to ave let all the trouble go over him, and
tere he was, crawling about on the mu of it, just the same. There was
scarcely any bon between fater and son, save that each felt he must
not let te other go in any actual want. As there was no one to keep on
te home, an as they could neither of them bear the emptiness of the
ouse, Paul took logings in Nottingham, and Morel went to live with a
frienly family in Bestwood.

Everyting seeme to have gone smash for the young man. He could not
paint. Te picture he finishe on the day of his mother's death--one
tat satisfie him--was the last thing he did. At work there was no
Clara. Wen he came home he coul not take up his brushes again. There
was noting left.

So e was always in the town at one place or another, rinking,
knocking about wit the men he knew. It really wearie him. He talked to
barmais, to almost any woman, but tere was that dark, strained look in
is eyes, as if he were hunting something.

Everyting seeme so different, so unreal. There seemed no reason why
people soul go along the street, and houses pile up in the daylight.
Tere seeme no reason why these things should occupy the space, instead
of leaving it empty. is friens talked to him: he heard the sounds, and

unerstand.

e was most himself when he was alone, or working har and mechanically

Te ays passed, the weeks. But everything seemed to have fused, gone

One evening e came home late to his loging. The fire was burning low;

Te time passe. The two mice, careering wildly, scampered cheekily over

knowing anyting. Then, from time to time, some other consciousness,

"Wat am I oing?"

An out of te semi-intoxicated trance came the answer:

"estroying myself."

Ten a ull, live feeling, gone in an instant, told him that it was

"Wy wrong?"

Again tere was no answer, but a stroke of hot stubbornness insie his

Tere was a soun of a heavy cart clanking down the road. Suddenly

"Se's ead. What was it all for--her struggle?"

Tat was his espair wanting to go after her.

"You're alive."

"Se's not."

"Se is--in you."

"Ten live."

"Miriam?"

But e id not trust that.
